RATING: N/A
Variant: First to 20 combined D/S wins; no need to return to Baghdad. Only one status per character. 

Essential house rule: change the daytime every time the first player begins his/her turn again. Otherwise, you will be stuck in the morning phase throughout the entire game and won't be able to reach and experience many of the book's encounters, as running through the whole encounter deck (which normally triggers the change of daytime by the official rules) literally never happened in my many games with 2-4 players.

But I really dislike Tales of the Arabian Nights.  Perhaps I only dislike it because it pales in comparison to similar games.  Maybe my expectations were misplaced.

RATING: 6
I understood going in that this was less of a game and more of an interactive story so I was still looking forward to it.  However, it ended up being better in concept than execution.

The game just feels too random and tedious to be fun.  We played a 3 player HALF game and didn't even finish in 2 hours!  

I didn't care that much about winning but even then it feels impossible to even make choices that will advance the game period.  You end up wandering aimlessly around when you even do get a choice.

The stories were fun, but choices that would seem to make sense end up randomly punishing you so that you feel like you made the "wrong" choice instead of a "fun" choice.

I'd play again, but definitely not purchasing this.  Try before you buy!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is a really special game. As countless others have stated before me, it is less of a game and more of an experience.

The unique strength of this game is that most turns involve some fundamental change to the character's fate and, over the course of a game, it does feel like life... not life when things make sense but life when it throws you a sidewinder and you live through it and forget it and then look back at it and say, "was that really still me?"

Those are the kind of changes that this game abounds with. Another aspect of it that is unique is that most of the decisions that your character has to make are not "how" decisions but "what" decisions. This brings the game-play experience closer to "who do I want my character to be" and further from "how do I get what I want". 

There is now a slew of story-oriented / reading games on the horizon but, from what I've read about them, they do not offer this kind of radical freedom. 

My only complaint is that the point-system can distract from all of the above - if ever you think you can somehow 'play' the game, that's when the experience can die down... and that temptation does arise from time to time. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Strategy be damned! Sometimes you just need to go on a roller coaster ride and see what happens to you. Tales of Arabian Nights shines in the story-telling department, bringing exciting tales of wild success along with depressing and hilarious tales of woe. 

With just the slightest "game" structure added for motivation, Tales is really an activity-in-disguise. But that matters not when there are thousands of different story snippets to guide the variety of put-upon heroes, such as Aladdin and Sinbad. Another game that is important to play with a crowd of people just looking to have fun, Tales excels at weaving strange and unpredictable stories.

Add a little extra flavor to the game by having one player make sound effects during a story reading, while another player acts out the events of the unfolding event.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
First play was with two - and it was very fun. Played in about 90 minutes. I recall playing this in the 80's and loving it then - although it played itself out after awhile. I think that might happen again, but for now, I'm looking forward to our next play. It's great fun.

Second play was with three - and played in about 2 hours. Still very fun - and quite the variety of things happen. It's very much an adventure and nice for a change of pace.

Third play was with four and at times, five (one fellow left). The game didn't know when to get off the table and there was no way to speed that along. Getting thrown in jail is truly annoying and then later, completely easy to get out - the one place that is repetitive in the tome. This one is probably just a holiday game now - bring it out once or twice a year at most.

And then it has left my collection. Regrets? Not really. More of a fondness now than actually wanting to play again. It is at times amazingly fun. Then it is amazingly not.

RATING: 8.5
Traducido al castellano, megacurrada de Decanox de Asylum Games! Reglas + MEGALIBRACO! Es un juego único en su especie. Una fusión de juego de mesa con juego de rol. Y se puede jugar casi con cualquiera, que guste del género narrativo y aventura. Recuerda en parte a aquellos libros de "elige tu propia aventura" :)
Se recomienda hasta 4 jugadores o menos. Con más, hace el entre-turno demasiado largo. Recomendado usar ciertas variantes que mejoran el juego.

Te mueves por el mapa, tienes encuentros en las localizaciones y en función del tipo de acción que elijas & las habilidades que dispongas  lees un párrafo en concreto con lo que sucede & que consecuencias tiene para tu personaje, pudiendo encontrar tesoros, aumentar o conseguir más habilidades, estados mentales, físicos o sociales positivos o negativos, aumentar o disminuir tu estatus de riqueza y conseguir puntos de destino y puntos de historia, que la combinación positiva de ambos y regresar a Bagdad, es lo que hace ganar la partida. Toda una experiencia de juego a disfrutar!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
If there is one thing I repeatedly argue about with the Other Player, it is the inability of certain board games to actually tell the story they are trying to sell. Well, here comes the King of all story telling games that all others will have to compare themselves to. 
Playing Tales is as beautiful as the eyes of Sheherazade, as exciting as a trip on Aladdins magic carpet, as mystical as the dark grove in Sindbads adventures. While playing, you can easily forget that it is indeed not a cooperative game. You may actually forget you're playing a board game. Tales is much more like a friendly introduction to role playing games.
If there is on tiny thing I would wish for if a magical Genie turned up and asked me about my whishes, I would ... naturally speak to him about something entirely unrelated to board games. But if, say, the designer of Tales 2.0 climbed out of that lamp, I would probably ask him for a digital version of the story book, because shuffling through the pages all the time really gets annoying after a while. But really, this feels like complaining about the color of the fabric after someone gave you a flying carpet for Christmas.

RATING: 8
FANTASTIC game for groups that enthusiastically commit to role playing their characters and dive into immersion gaming practices. Also great for storytellers, readers and creative minds alike. 

People who won't like this game are those who like quick play games, do this, then that, then add up points type gamers. There's a lot of dice rolling so less strategy than many would prefer. Also, people who are closed minded to various world cultures and religions would suffer tremendously through this brilliant game. Those who want to learn more about folklore of the "others", well, this is a win.  Those offended by "Allah" best pick a different title.  Or better yet, play it and read some factual unbiased material before/after to become educated on the good (oft forgotten toward those) who do know "Allah". It's really a minor part of the game but enough it will create disdain for extremists. 

For me and my family, we deeply enjoy enriching our minds, hearts and lives with other cultures, ways and beliefs AND we tremendously devour creativity and imagination type adventures.  Naturally this game was a big hit at our home.

RATING: 8
A great story telling game, but not one for actual "gameplay". As an experience it is fantastic, especially if you can get into a character and go with the story. I recently played a game as an attractive merchant, who sold his wares into two far off cities, then upset a Djin who banished him to a far off land. He then met some people who wanted to tease a vizier, who wasn't happy about it, and sent him to jail. I then spent the rest of the game trying to bribe, attack and converse with the guards to no escape. 
The game is random, but so long as you go along for the ride and accept this, it'll be a fun time. There really is nothing like it.
I wouldn't say this is one that will always be brought out, but when it is, you are in for an evening of amusing tales.

[b]Solo Variant[/b] - [filepage=122305]Tales of the Arabian Nights Official Solo Variant [/filepage] with Ideas from [thread=1093637]Alex F's Thread[/thread] and [thread=1355895]Matt Rice's Thread[/thread]
[b]Cooperative Variant[/b] - [thread=1439356]Tales of the Arabian Nights Cooperative Variant[/thread] by [user=dugfromthearth]DugFromTheEarth[/user]

:thumbsup: There is a unique narrative that develops for each character with every play, keeping the variability quite high.
:thumbsup: Plotting a character's path around the world is a very enjoyable puzzle that constantly changes.

[microbadge=30754][c] [/c]The very random nature of most encounters and paragraphs takes away a lot of strategic thinking.
[microbadge=30754][c] [/c]Parts of the game board text are upside down or sideways, which is an annoyance for a solo player.

:thumbsdown: Almost everything is unpredictable, yet certain elements can feel repetitive and stale rather quickly.
:thumbsdown: There is a very large amount of required table space, and maneuvering the Book of Tales is cumbersome.

RATING: 6
Right - straight off. This isn't a game.

It's a storytelling experience for several players to go through independently at the same time.

The decision of what score you're aiming for is meaningless as there's very little indication in the rules or on the cards what your actions might score. 

Skills are also a luck based thing - you may use one skill repeatedly, or get no skills used at all without any basis in your decisions.

That being said, it's a great fun way to spend an evening!

The board and pieces are functional and beautiful in equal measure.

I'd like it in trade to while some time away with the other half...

RATING: 8
Great 'experience' game where winning isn't really the end goal although it is there if you need it to be. Create your own narrative to a unique story as you play. The game play is surprisingly simple allowing you to sit back and enjoy the fun unlike my other favorite experience game, Magic Realm. The game works with kids for the most part as well as non-gamers to some extent although you often will get some confusion over the concept of a game without a real need to win. The components in this version are very nice and the game is well worth the price. Lots of fun.

RATING: 6
I recognize that this should not be rated as a game, or even approached as a game, but rather as an experience, and that's what rates it a 6 for me. Thematically, it's wonderful, and the art is great. And there is SOOOOO much bad luck in it, that it is worth playing just for laughs. It's a fun experience, but not a great experience. I like the theme.....but I'd probably rate it higher with a different theme (like Norse mythology, or the Chronicles of Narnia). I might also rate it higher if it didn't take so long. For me to play something like this for such a long time, it would have to have FAR more tension to hold my interest, and it is not at all tense. I can't understand at all why it is rated so highly here, but I suppose RPG fans would dearly love it. I am not an RPG fan, but I have certainly played much more interesting RPG's.

RATING: 6
*Revised from 7 to 6 due to new storytelling games casting shade on what's starting to feel like a more dated game.

At least 60 minutes per player, unless you house-rule the points cap downwards to speed things up. Feels WAY too long without that change, and you will probably never get through the deck to reach the 'afternoon/night' phase of the cards unless you also house-rule that encounter deck down to a set number of cards. I strongly recommend implementing both.
By the end of a game, I was a crippled scorned insane beastperson, and still loved the game. A game for existentialism, not strategy. Unfortunately, play depth vs play time is not a good balance.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Less of a competitive game than a shared choose your own adventure.  The story book is a work of genius, the theme is fantastic, and the play is fairly smooth once you figure out the encounter mechanics.  The fun comes from your group and how it plays together, although the game has ample suggestions for interesting/comedic reactions to situations.  There's a bit of down time between turns if you have more than 3 players, so you might want a drink or two in hand.

Update: Once you have played a few times, it gets really old.  I made it a point to get to the dusky land, and I've been twice with limited success.  There are better ways to spend a long session, although it's still a good activity for non-gamers.

RATING: 1
Not a game, but a hostage situation. The lack of any actual, meaningful choice is stunning, and even when an action seems completely logical you may wind up hamstrung by your choice.

I played a single game, and that was enough. "Grief-Stricken" from turn one, I could do nothing throughout the entire 3+ hours it took to plod through this crapfest. Further negative status effects meant that my actions were predetermined by the game and that someone else made all my other decisions for me, including where I moved on the map, so even while the game's choices are completely arbitrary I didn't get to make a single one of them.

You can tell me that I got unlucky, that the chances of getting so screwed are remarkably slim, that it'll be better next time, but there won't be a next time. The simple fact that the game would have been no different had I left the table, and that I was taken out of the game from the very first turn, means that this is not any sort of actual game. A fun activity? Maybe for some. As for me, I'd rather perform DIY dental surgery or play house-rules Monopoly with a bunch of PCP-intoxicated bonobos.

RATING: 8.6
The production values here are great, with a wonderful board, cards, and pieces, plus the massive Tales book which you will be referring to as your characters move through this ancient world. It's an interactive story telling game, though that interaction is in how the players narrate eat others tales; sort of like a massive multiplayer Choose Your Own Adventure book but with a board component and a minimal rules structure to make it slightly more gamey. You'll keep track of your movement, Story and Destiny points (each player hides his ultimate goal), and the rewards you get from Encounters and completing Quests. I do really like the simplistic Skills tile system, offers some added variety each time you play. Arabian Nights is a lot of fun, but just know what you're getting into here.

RATING: 10
You can't beat nostalgia. This was the first serious board game I ever played - well, the '80s version was. I had resigned myself to never having it myself, when I learned that Z-Man was remaking it. It was an instant buy in California, and we had to pack it in for our flight home!

Maybe even role play a bit? The Book of Tales is just the beginning, and it doesn't have all the answers - but it certainly poses the right questions.

RATING: 3
"will play sporadically if in the right mood" prefectly describes my attitude towards this "game".  (I gave it a bump to 6.5 because it can be a great time with a good crowd)

As others have said, Tales isn't really a game but more of an "experience".  Which is a shame because I think there are probably ways to keep the primary ideas behind it (a story-telling / choose-your-own-adventure style production) but still give players a bit more control over their destiny.

As long as players have the right expectations this is a fine (and lavishly produced) title.  I can't help thinking that it could have been a home run if it had a bit more "game" in it.

UPDATE:
Ultimately Tales is a "beautiful failure".  I respect its scope and ambition but it falls short of its goal -- even if you agree not to evaluate this as a "game" and more a story telling experience.

Stories are supposed to be focused and purposeful.  Character's usually should have goals and this gives their actions meaning.

Because the gameplay in Tales is so weak, the character's dont really have goals to speak of or any challenge to overcome.  Nothing really drives their decisions.  Not only does this make for a bad game (which fans will say is not the point of Tales), but it makes for dreadful stories (which is supposedly the whole point)!

Mostly Tales is just one perplexing schadenfreude gag after another.  It's a collection of surprising prat-falls with no actual narrative to string them together.

plus...despite a huge book of tales, you still get a surprising amount of repeats (even in a single session).  "you got beaten by monkeys...again"

Tales is a non-game that generates boring, non-sensical stories.

RATING: 2
First Play: The production quality is excellent and the story book and matrix took me back to my D'n'D days.

But.........

This isn't a game unless you class snakes and ladders as a credible game. Apart from the judgement required to move your character, you can input no meaningful strategy into your turn. You select skills, you encounter a situation, you decide whether you have a relevant action that supports one of these skills, you make an informed choice, roll a die and the corresponding passage is read from the master tome. In all cases your skill has no bearing on the subsequent turn of events and you receive a random allocation of points and perhaps a treasure.

The random flip of encounters just adds to the chaos. 

Our 6 player game lasted 4 hours with enormous down time between turns when you have nothing to do or consider.

It started off as a little fun but after 2 hours I was willing the experience to end but could see that the points gained were no where near.

If this game had been around 30 years ago when the Fighting Fantasy books had come out it would have been a smash hit. Now it is just a lame passive slog through a series of random die rolls and equally chaotic illogical responses that decide what points, hindrances or benifits are given to each player.

I really should give this a 1 as I never want to play again and don't think its a game, but we did laugh from time to time, but what a waste of 4 hours.

RATING: 9
I have no doubt this will be a treasured life-long possession. There aren't many games that can pack this much magic into a box. Definitely worth every cent of the 100+ bucks I paid for it. It's great for bonding with friends, as you'll always come away with things to laugh about together and stories to tell. To really enjoy it, though, you'll need to forget about making meaningful strategic decisions, since no matter the choose-your-own-adventure choices you make, you can be led in any number of unpredictable directions. It's not a game that you play; it's actually the game that plays YOU. Just strap in to your magic carpets and enjoy the ride! And here are the house rules that I play with: 1) Change the morning/midday/night tokens after every round, instead of when the card deck runs out. More variability that way. 2) Each player holds only one status card at a time. Two is okay too, but any more than that and it starts to get too unweildy and confusing. 3) Men are able to court men, and women are able to court women. The fact that same-sex romance was banned in the original rules is just anachronistic rubbish. 4) Having a sex-change does NOT prevent you from winning the game. Again, that's just outdated dinosaur values getting in the way of a good game. Solidarity to all the beautiful transfolk out there.

RATING: 6
This game is not about winning at all, because there's very little you can do to strategize. It's really about creating a story. 

Every turn you perform a small action and then you face a random encounter and decide how to react. How do you react to a crazy beggar? Do you hire them? Beat and rob them? Question them? Maybe they're a dethroned prince and you can help them or maybe they're just an old man. You really have no idea, but it'll be a great story. 

There's an android app to help you parse through the gigantic book of all these scenarios that's useful to have.

RATING: 3
I REALLY wanted to like this.  I was hoping to play it with my family.  Beautiful components.  Looks very nice.

2 problems
1 . its essentially a solitaire game where every turn you roll to see if you succeed in the space you enter.  You can easily spend a few turns in the same space.  You will get some good things here and there (treasures) and some curses.  Some of thos ecurses will really screw you, and you might never get rid of them.  Your decisions are worth little, except in some very obvious cases.  (dont drink from a poison lake).  I had a skill that I was a master at, I chose a course of action that emphasized it, in an appropraite situation, and got screwed.

2. It takes 2 hours!!!  Given its nature, if the game took 30-45 minutes I'd rate it a lttle better.

There was nothing really fun about it after 30 minutes.
It seems way outdated and there are much more fun games avaialable.
Its Beautiful, but far too long for a mediocre solitaire game.  Play someone elses copy one time, and you will see what I am talking about.  Also - too much down time with many players, but since its random and decisions dont matter who cares.

RATING: 3.5
Is this a game?  It's probably better described as a way to pass time because it feels like the game is playing you.  Effectively, this is a race game with a lot of luck from cards and dice rolling.  With 5p, it took way too long to play.  Often you take a turn, accomplish very little, then wait 15 minutes for another turn.  In order to do well in the game, you need to have the correct "talent" tokens for random encounters; however, the talents (aside from starting talents) are usually obtained by chance or luck... and the talents that are helpful for a given encounter often do not make intuitive sense.  There are many other games that I would rather spend 2-3 hours playing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
It breaks my heart to rate this a four. What an incredible theme. What a beautifully produced game. How beautifully the rulebook is written, with so much work and affection for the source material. As a game, though (at least one I'd like to play), it just doesn't hold together. The decisions just don't matter. It's "Choose your own adventure" in a group, waiting for each person to go until you finally make your decision. And your decision doesn't matter. You need to track the changes to your character. They don't matter. You need to keep track of cards affecting your character which don't matter either. My heart is completely in sympathy (empathy, even) with those who define "game" more broadly and present "Tales" as a storytelling game more than a strategy game. Here's the thing, I don't think it succeeds there, either. The paragraphs are too well defined and too specific to encourage the kind of narrative play that I've heard the game can promote. See "Once Upon a Time" for a more constructive option there. I hope one day I can find a niche for this game in my life as I'm strangely glad that I own it - I just can't get it to work as I wished it did.

The book fascinated me, so I kept it in the back of my mind as something to check for myself one day.

Now I have a copy and I understand why he didn't really want to get into back then.  This can be a lot of fun, but a full game seems to take about an hour per player.  And in the end, though someone wins, the outcome seems largely random.

Still I like it, not so much as a competitive game, but as a pastime when I got time to spare and have no particular interest in something else to do.

You encounter a strange arcane shop filled with many boxes painted bright cheerful colors.  One of the larger and heavier boxes catches your eye. It has a picture of a djiin and the legendary sailor Sinbad etched across the top.

NO SKILL: You look inside but do not understand the grandeur involved in the strange cardboard and large spiral bound book.  The shop keeper instead edges you towards Abstracts and Eurogames with defined goals and strategies. [S:1]

ENDURING HARDSHIP: A large man comes into the shop and says "Put that down, I had it on preorder."  He pays for it and quickly runs off. Not to be denied your prize, you follow him to an area with chairs and tables where other people await him.  They quickly punch out many cardboard chits and pull out a map of Arabia.  They pretend to be heros of myth, like Sinbad and Aladin.  They pass a large book around and dramatically roll dice in order to have numbers told to them by another of the gathering.  They seem to be enjoying themselves, but their choices seem to have little bearing on the outcomes of these short stories and one player seems to be constantly beating a Hunchback in order to try to escape from being in prison while another complains that being a sex-changed, beast-form, grief-stricken and married isn't making things very fun for them.  One of them eventually causes an event that makes them put everything back into the box to a chorus of 'good game'.  You walk away confused. [S:1, D:1, [i]Confused[/i] ]

STORYTELLING: You open the box and believe you have found the suitcase of Marcelas Wallace. Contained therein are colorful chits and a map of Arabia. A tome, weighing many pounds, has many thousands of paragraphs of entertainment and adventure to be shared with your friends.  You quickly empty your pockets and whisk this new found treasure to your next game meeting, where you accept the fact that you cannot control the fates, but are only along for the ride.  You delight in the storytelling, acting out the characters of an enraged djiin or a crazed beggar. Your friends enjoy themselves as well and are glad you made this purchase.  [S:1, D:1, W:-1 (no minimum), [i]Beguiled[/i]]

WEAPON USE: You draw forth your scimitar and slay the mighty dragon, shouting praises to Allah. A small green creature from outerspace chances upon you and says "I believe this paragraph is a typo and should have been #2583.  Sorry about that." [S:2, D:1, [i]Typographical Error[/i]]

RATING: 8
This was certainly completely different than anything else we've played. It's actually a pretty impressively put together game, and you can tell a lot of careful work was put into developing it.

I ended up winning our first game, but I can't exactly say that I had much of a role in that happening. I suppose there are a few decisions to make (doing attack oriented things when you have a weapon, for example), but that doesn't always work out how you expect. So, it seemed pretty random to me, and 2 or 3 particularly good fortune events helped give me take a solid lead in the middle of the game.

It was fun seeing the stories develop, and the status cards really are well designed. I would certainly play this again, and would be interested to see how replayable it is. Initial rating an 8 due to quality and enjoyment, although as a competitive game it really can't be compared to other titles.

RATING: 7
In some ways, I wasn't expecting too much from this game, mostly because it was a reboot of an 80's classic (the reason I got it, never having played the original).  Those "classic" games from the 80s often are more fun to think about playing than to actually play  (I'm looking at you, Avalon Hill).  I thought it would be fun once in a while, a game to be played when the mood hits you, but which you wouldn't want to exhaust.  After more than a few plays, the game seems pretty well inexhaustible - we've hit repeat encounters a few times, and probably will hit them more the more we play, but really, they blended right in with the action.  Stringing together the episodes into a tale (of misery, usually, in my case) is fun and funny.  Ahhh yes, unless you've been a ravishing woman turned into a hideous ape, forced to wander, crippled, blind, and lost, infected with a horrible STD by a hideous hunchback, well, you might not know what I'm talking about.  The production values of this game are second to none, natch for Z-Man, and the game can be played quite handily in 90 min.  Yes, this has already hit the table more than I thought, and there are many mechanics you can control (subtle thought they be).  Get this game - now.  For around $35 at online retailers, you cannot go wrong.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Utterly hilarious, ridiculous storytelling boardgame. It does remind me of those old choose-your-own-adventure books, as well as some of the old text-based computer RPGs I used to play as a kid. The events are arbitrary, players have little to no control over the outcomes of their decisions, and there is not even a hint of strategy or tactical decision-making, which obviously rankles some of the more uptight users on BGG. 

The game is meant to be experienced, however, not to be won. (If you play this game to win there is seriously something wrong with you). We were laughing so hard when my wounded, crippled, grief-stricken and insane hero tried to limp his way to complete his pilgrimage before having to head home to check in with his wife. Being married totally sucks in this game.

With that said, we were lucky to have finished in three hours. The game is just too long, especially because of the number of cards that prevent you from winning even when you have enough points. I love the idea of the game, I admire the amount of work that went into writing all of that text, and I definitely won't trade the game away anytime soon, but I will probably only play it a couple of times a year. We may try to shorten the game by setting the victory conditions a little lower.

RATING: 9
UPDATE: I do like this game a lot. But it's probably another game I am fated to like a lot more than most people who share most of my other gaming tastes (see also El Capitan).

After our last game, Kim had an intriguing suggestion for tightening the screws just a touch on this: start players with 4 or even 5 skills instead of 3. This would let players hit the ground running instead of having the slightly slow early game as it is, where players both can't accomplish too much until they get a break, and where some debilitating conditions can be quite nasty and lead you to end up being stuck.
---
I thoroughly enjoyed this game and felt like it did a ton of stuff right. I thought had good narrative arc, not just for the obvious reasons, but because players gained skill and wealth at a generally good clip, allowing them to develop and exert more control over the encounter matrices as the game went on. It wasn't too long, at 2 hours-ish. It's a bit mechanical with all the page-flipping, which is a bummer, but once you get into the groove it's not all that bad. And the stories are great.

But, and it's a big but, some of the statuses you can get stuck with really suck. Insane is good for a laugh for a few turns but gets old quickly. Ensorcelled is just awful and sucks the fun out of the game very rapidly. Scorned is a flavorful irritant the first time, but it seems to get handed out a fair amount and having to deal with it more than once also sucks. Maybe there is more control that you can exert if you are familiar with the flavor of the game, but you don't want to strip the players of the couple real choices they make each turn.

I think it also helps if you have some sense of the flavor of the game and what choices it is likely to reward.

We played with four, two of us really enjoyed it, two of us less so. A lot depends on whether you can feel like you are making some progress. Obviously, it's a somewhat random game, and if it works out that you can't, it can be not fun, especially given the length. 2-2.5 hours in 1985 was not a big deal, these days, it a little more on the outside edge. 4 players was decent, 3 is probably the sweet spot, and I would definitely not play with 5 or 6.

Regardless, I thought it was a lot of fun, was an evocative and flavorful game, and would happily play again. But it clearly won't be for everyone.

RATING: 7
Tales of the Arabian Nights is such a hard game to rate!  I absolutely love it when I do play it, but then once I play it I'm pretty much done with it for a year until I want to play it again, and I'm even kind of tired of it by the time I finish the game.  I think it's because Tales is more of an activity than a game.  It's basically a choose-your-own-adventure exercise with points being semi-randomly distributed and stuff happening to you that you really don't have a heck of a lot of control over.  It makes for a really fun story telling experience although in the end, it's not really a game so much as an activity.  An activity that, for me, is great fun maybe once a year.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Sure, there isn't much "game" here. It's totally random and defies any strategies applied to it. You wander around the world, make choices in a "choose your own adventure" style,  and things happen.

However, this box is magical. The stories you will create are bustling with wonder and amazement. It's very difficult to explain, but the way the enormous storybook (filled with over 2000 different encounters) is designed so that every response to your choices makes sense...the fact that this game works at all is pure sorcery.

It's simple. It takes a few minutes to get it on the table and explain the rules. There's very little downtime since the game smartly gives three players something to do each round.

RATING: 6
Reading about this I was extremely excited, however when I finally brought it to the table no-one was having fun. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
SPECIAL NOTE: Make sure you house-rule the hell out of this game to add to the narrative and RPG aspects. If someone is stuck with a lousy status half of the game, think of a clever way to help them get rid of it; read in character; don't try to make the objectively 'best' decision, rather make decisions in character; play to fewer victory points; make great stories together. It's a unique board-game and unbeatable for what it does. A word of warning though: If you're all about maximizing and winning, this game may not be for you (I'm usually about maximizing and winning though, and I love it). And yes, it is random as hell, so enjoy the ride (and the crazy amount of work that went into the design.

After second play: I REALLY, REALLY like this game. It isn't a competitive 'game' in any sense that would satisfy a person who is looking for that sort of thing, but as a collaborative story-telling adventure it is unsurpassable. The fact that it adds some 'game' aspects to what is basically interactive story-telling is icing on the cake. If it covered a theme of more interest to me (Homeric Greece or Wild West or...the possibilities are endless), I would probably just pass-out into a drooly, orgasmic puddle. My game design retirement project: use this system to develop a game with an even cooler theme. Top 10. Love it. 

Great storytelling and tons of fun. I prefer playing to lower victory point totals, and perhaps giving each player an extra skill because it tends to drag (especially if you get stuck with nasty statuses that you can't shake--that's part of the fun though right?). It's very different from any other games that I've played and must be considered a cooperative storytelling-fun-rpg type of experience, rather than a competitive game; you're telling stories together. Given those expectations, and a lower winning totals to keep the playing time reasonable, I can't imagine a more enjoyable social game. Newbies should enjoy it a lot, just don't play with too many people (5 or 6 would drag I think) and consider playing to a lower point total. Fun, fun, fun creative story-making that is all about what you bring to it and make of it. I need more games like this (too bad there aren't any; so sad).

RATING: 4
Based on 1 play with 4 players:

This game is something of an oddity. Players don't have a lot of control, there's almost no meaningful decision making (especially since all skills are essentially the same), and no real player interaction... and yet it somehow manages to remain engaging, even over about two hours. You just want to listen in to other people's stories.

Lots of chaos and luck, so I could see how this could easily drag on.

Still if you don't mind a game that's less like a game and more like a group activity with fun storytelling elements, this is one of the better ones around.

I suspect this is best with 3 and that my rating would go up or down dramatically if I played again.

You will like this game if you can handle absurdity with laughter instead of with frustration.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Tales of Arabian nights is a great game, but it gets a 7 because it's not the type of game that I will always want to play.  

It's essentially a large interactive choose your own adventure story, which you're completing simultaneously with other players.  

The choices you make are more or less illusions, because while it may seem like a good idea to make one choice or another in a given encounter, you're never really sure of the outcome and things more or less just happen to you whether you want them to or not.  (Hey! Just like real life!)

Nevertheless, it's an enjoyable, albeit long experience.  It does have an enormous amount of replay value because there are various quests and story paths to pursue.  I imagine I could play this dozens of times and still come up with a different character destiny each time.  

What's a little odd is how the game is won under these conditions.  Eventually a player will accumulate enough story and destiny (victory points) to win the game.  Unless that player specifically had a random negative status assigned to them during the game, the other players pretty much can't do anything about it. 

So while the game is fun, in my view it loses points because there's less player interaction, cooperation, or competition.  In fact, the most interactive you get is just passing the story book and decision matrix tool around so that the players on your left and right help read the outcomes of your decision. 

Thus, this is not a high skill game or a way to flex your mental prowess.  It's a good for people who appreciate a good story, or kids, and you can laugh and cry at the luck and misfortune of your character and the others in the game.

You can also fall into a state of not being able to do anything for quite a long time without any way out of it except for a random lucky draw. I do really like the idea of the game... the execution just isn't interesting enough for me.

RATING: 8
This narrative-driven "experiential" game was much more fun than I expected. Its a little procedural in the constant looking up tables and paragraphs, but once you get into the routine of that the game flows pretty quickly.

The theme holds up extremely well. You're constantly making small choices, but for the most part your fate is out of your hands. There isn't a whole lot of direct character-character interaction, but it feels like there is plenty of player interaction because you look up and read text for the players next to you. 

The game provided lots of laughs, and ended just when it was starting to feel a little long. I would gladly play this again. I'm looking forward to seeing how it goes over with grade-school aged kids.

RATING: 6
(Play with streamlining variants including only allowing 1 status at a time and playing to 20 combined score, min 5 in each to avoid cheese wins with a status that prevents scoring in one of them, no pre-game choose as it just slows the game down).

Tales of the Arabian Nights isn't really so much a game as it is an amusing activity with a board, a book, and some dice to spice things up. I can't really rate it highly as a game, because you really aren't making decisions that I would consider all that gamey at any point in the proceedings. Yes, you can make some choices based on their perceived value to your point scoring goals (if you play with those: I find the game random enough to make choosing them not worth the trouble, YMMV) but what happens is so up to the vagueries of fate that there isn't much point in thinking things out logically beyond the obvious things: like I have a lot of money or negotiation skills, so choices that might use those in resolution seem reasonably more likely to work.

So yeah, you wander Arabia, you have encounters, you read stuff out of the book, and eventually someone wins. It's not really role-playing per se, though you can definitely get into amusing story-telling mode if you're the reader. And it's not really gamey as there is usually an obvious path you should be taking to score points (i.e.: your quest). All of that said, I find it fun as a once in a while activity. That also said, the game can take a while, and if you play it with more than 4 you are going to be hurting for time. 

I think this also benefits from a GM type arrangement and I am generally willing to run the game for other interested players in that capacity, as I think a good story-teller with some familiarity with the game can both speed it up and make it more fun for casual players; this is particularly the case given the way I play it with only 1 status allowed, as there is room to come up with funny reasons why after encounter X you are no longer Y but are now Z. I would have also liked some more ways for the players to interact, but given that this is based on a very old title and how narrative driven it is I can understand the way it was formatted. Great flavour throughout and fun for what it is, though you definitely have to be in the specific niche for this to enjoy it at all.

RATING: 8
This is not in the truest sense a "game" that you want to "win."  It's actually an experience for the group as the whole as you listen to tales of triumph and woe.  Perhaps you can work together to help solve each other's problems.  Or perhaps you'll set off on a solo quest.  The choices are truly yours.

This is full of so many stories and variations, it's hard to imagine getting bored with this game.  As a fan of "Choose Your Own Adventure" fiction as a kid, this tickles my fancy.  In a way it's an evolution of the party game - a big leap towards thematic games like Arkham Horror or BSG.

A lot of thought has gone into the design of this game and it really shows.  Worth its nearly 8# in weight!  The only negatives would be a little downtime when you're not reading or taking your turn, and the game can get quite long when you have more players or rotten luck.  But for a rainy day where you're not in a rush, who cares?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It fails miserably as a game, as you have virtually no control over your ultimate fate. But as an activity it really is quite enjoyable, with a well-paced development arc and interesting emergent stories (like the time I decided to attack everything in sight, many djinns included, with my Master Weapon Use and got Beast Formed three separate times). This is really something you have to play with the right group; competitive players will find this distasteful, and it extends a bit too long for most players that don’t have a specific interest in adventure games. Three seems to be the right target number of this, too, as it minimizes downtime, with everyone having something to do on a turn.

Within that context, though, there are still some things that really nag. Quests are poorly balanced, with some completely up to the whims of fate, others so time-consuming as to take longer than a full game, and a few way too easy. Likewise, the method for advancing through time is broken; I can’t see how anyone could ever progress into night or even midday as written. But the most frustrating part is some of the statuses. It’s not that many encounters will randomly stick you with negative statuses, as this is simply part of the fun of the game; it’s that some of the effects they laden you with tend to suck the fun out of the game. An early Grief-Stricken robs you of even your limited choices for an hour or so; Sex-Changed prevents you from winning and is very difficult to get rid of; a lingering Ensorcelled is a real negative play experience; and so on. Alternative ways to lose these by paying story and destiny would have been a real bonus.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Components: Attractive board, with a few tiny details to make it seem inhabited (including different symbols for the cities on different continents). Clear paths between locations. Quality cards. The Book of Tales is spiral bound and sturdy. This game would greatly benefit from an app to handle some of the randomization.

Experience: What a cool experience. There aren't any strategic choices, but there is a fair amount of deduction, as I try to interpret the situation and guess which reaction will lead to an encounter where I can favorably use my skills. However, the paragraphs need to have more skills available to them, as I seldom have an applicable skill.

Though the various encounters don't chain together very well, there is some carry-over in the form of treasures, statuses, skills, and quests. I'd like more of an overarching narrative.

The storytelling is a delight. Well-written passages, with a variety of circumstances. This is exactly what I was looking for when I came to it, to be immersed in a fun story. This game is so clever, and I am amazed at the care that went into its creation.

Some of the statuses are particularly harsh (Imprisoned, Sex-Changed); others are cumbersome to use (Respected). Treasures are seldom meted out. I like that the Quests, location encounter cards, and a few other mechanisms provide me geographic direction for where I should head.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
My issue with the game arises from the way you win: you must hit 20 points in the combination of the Destiny/Story points that you chose. So if you choose 13 Destiny and 7 Story [i]and[/i] you eventually hit those numbers, you win. (very simplified)

This is all well and good. But to me, there was something weird about this mechanic. Basically you are unable to control where the points go, they are dictated to you and almost impossible to foretell which answer will get you which points.

So, as I said above, I've been thinking and took some things into account.

1) The day/evening/night markers. Sag told us (stormseeker75 and I) that he had never seen anything but the day be used and even commented along the lines "I have no idea how you can even get to the next marker, perhaps 6 player."
2) The sheer number of [destiny cards] - I can't remember what they are... but every player receives one and gives you some idea of what to do in the game.
3) The choice of the winning combination. There was no way to insure you getting more story if you needed it or vise versa.

My conclusion:

I'm wondering if a mistake has been made in dealing out the Destiny/Story points. (not by the game we played but in general)

When you complete an event successfully on the paragraph it might say:

+1D/+2S/+2W (which translates +1 Destiny point and +2 Story points and +2 Wealth)...

I wondering if it should really read: +1 Destiny point or +2 Story points or +2 Wealth.

This fixes an issue I had, namely being able to choose where your points go.

It would make the game go significantly longer... very much so [i]but[/i] it would be able to use all three of those time markers that I commented about above.

It is still a 8.5 for me though... simply great game.

See my review: http://boardgame.geekdo.com/thread/451176

RATING: 6
Players wander around most of the world encountering adventures.  Unique game where spaces combine with a card deck, die roll, and reaction choice to trigger an paragraph of adventure text from an enormous book of Tales.  This may instantly resolve, or lead you onto further adventures.  Adventure outcomes usually grant talents (good) and status (not always good), as well as treasure, destiny points and story points.  Destiny and Story are what you're trying to obtain to win back in Baghdad.  Fun game that really plays you.  If you're not prepared to strap in and enjoy the ride, you will hate it.  Otherwise, good fun.  The Z-man edition has to be the heaviest game per cubic centimeter in my collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A heavy game box containing high quality materials: map board, booklets, player aids, playing pieces and lots of cards. The map portrays our world along with places found in the 1000+1 nights’ fairy tale. Each player selects a character and chooses in secret how many points of Story and Destiny points will need to achieve his goal. Then he gets a mission and he is on his way to fulfill it. After every movement, each player reads a huge book and tries to find out an event that occurs to him taken from the famous fairy tale. Upon finding the event he has to take an action. The action has to be based on the logic of the fairy tale event. If successful he gets Story or Destiny points, if not, he can be jailed, transformed into another being by a wicked wizard, get enslaved, diseased, blessed, crippled, pursued, mad etc. Also during his adventures a player can upgrade, master or lose skills that might be useful in a future unpleasant encounter. Along your travels you can use magic carpets, teleport and find magic places. The game ends when a player has managed to reach the points he has secretly assigned. Fun, interactive and recreational.

RATING: 9
AKA "Schadenfreude with Scheherazade"

Played once and oh my goodness this is fun -- a great combination of a storytelling game and a "choose your own adventure" book, maybe with a little of Arkham Horror's overproduced joy. Get lost, imprisoned, married off, and turned into an ape all in one game. Don't expect to have some big strategy. Just adventure around and laugh at the misfortunes of others and they're going to laugh at yours.

Some of the statuses (statii?) are a real drag though (You can't win if you've been sex-changed and the only way to fix it is to get another sex change? Nuts to that.)

Also, the ramp-up time can get a little frustrating: you flounder around for a bit until you can start getting slightly better results in encounters. The next time we play I'll probably take some suggestions here and start with four or even five skills for each character.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I like Arkham Horror because it tells an interesting story and winning is not the primary goal of the game, however I wanted something that doesn't take as long to play and set up. Along comes Tales of the Arabian Nights. The game offers a rich story, however I find the rich story (Matrix N and Places of Power) will only happen if you or one of the other players randomly happens to have the correct skill relating to your encounter paragraph(s) or when a player randomly gains a treasure. There is very little game play and it's so random that playing to win is very pointless, but this is pretty much expected from a choose-your-own-adventure game. I think this would be a good gateway game for people too shy to play a pen & paper RPG. That being said, if you want a good story you would probably be better served reading a book or playing an RPG. If you want a game with story elements Arkham Horror would probably be a better choice than this since there is much more game play and strategy involved.

The components really left something to be desired when it came to functionality. The primary example would be a full flat worth of tokens to set your secret goal of Destiny and Story points. It's suggested you place them under your mat which is kind of silly, when pen & paper is quicker and more convenient. 

The extra flat could have been used for more skill chits because some of the skills only have 3 chits but the game plays up to six. I don't think it would be unrealistic to have 6 players get Piety throughout the game which means that multiple players would need to record this on a sheet of paper. I guess it's not a huge issue because I would never play this game with six but you still could run into this issue with less than 6. This same issue exists with having only 4 of any given status.

As for the Book of Tales, it has great stories in it, but why God, why did Z-Man choose a coiled spine?!  There are just so many things wrong with a coiled spine.  I also think the Encounter Charts in the beginning of the Book of Tales should have been separated into it's own chart reference like the Reaction Matrix. If they had decided to do it this way, it would allow 4 people to do something every round.

As mentioned earlier, I find the Places of Power and Matrix N encounters (if a character has the proper skill, so they can read up to about 5 or 6 paragraphs in a row) to be the most entertaining aspect of the game. The unfortunate thing is that these Place of Power encounters don't happen nearly enough in a given game and if they did, they may become old. People will be given Matrix N encounters more frequently than Places of Power, but usually will not have the proper skills to get multiple story paragraphs in a row.

Overall, this is a fairly enjoyable experience. Initially, I had great fun at the variety of the encounters and laughing at other people's misfortune with their negative statuses, but eventually it got a little old. I think this will be an amazing game for the right individual, but for me it's an average game with some poor component choices.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
It took me a couple years, but I finally broke down and played Tales of the Arabian Nights, a game on the opposite side of my taste dial. As it was explained to me, it was more of an experience than a game, and this is a good thing it didn't muck this up game mechanics and rules.

Essentially it is a choose-your-own-adventure book with a convoluted randomization system to get you to the next chapter. Mechanically each chapter (turn you take) just adds 0,1,2, or even negative points to your score, so the winner of the game is the one to be lucky enough to choose the right path. Meaning, don't play this is looking for any strategy.

As a story driven social experience it does a great job. I played Time Stories last year and was wishing for a stroke to take me out of my misery, Arabian Nights moved quickly, did not require too much reading (for those who are shy), and did weave a memorable story.

RATING: 9
This is very much an experience game - there is some skill involved, based on picking the best response to an encounter - but overall, things do happen and you can end up in a bad way through little fault of your own. It's not a competitive game either. The players are there more to share in your experiences than to compete and that's really the fun of TotAN. It's all about the theme, story development and just plain old adventuring! 

Nevertheless, things must be kept moving and downtime kept to an absolute minimum if this game is to work. Repetitiveness and downtime is the killer for games like these. 2 hours is the limit. Anything longer and the game enjoyment will head on a downward spiral. Thus, 4 players is the absolute upper limit, and it will help if everyone is into the theme.

RATING: 7.5
Choose-your-own-adventure and storytelling element makes this a lot of fun!  Very fun game!  Some minor issues: 
* Best with 2-3 players. with 4 or more down-time can start being an issue.  
* Replayability is a minor concern, some events happen quite often (e.g. Imprisoned) and kids can memorize some results, but overall you'll get your money's worth out of this game before you're tired of it.
* The stories and the game do have a decidedly Islamic flavour at times
* Rulebook does need some clarification [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/489237/why-do-i-have-more-rules-questions-about-this-game]at times[/url]
Overall, my kids have really enjoyed this immensely however.  

Suggested house rules:
- Rather than pick separate Story/Destiny points, have a cumulative total for players to aim for, e.g. 20 points.
- Divide Encounter deck into three parts, and use Morning, Noon, and Night for each (or use the Destiny dice to decide which it is).
- Allow a win when [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/426429/sex-changed-now-evil]sex changed[/url].

Idea for using minis: [thread=424696][/thread]

RATING: 6
A fairly interesting storytelling boardgame. The decision-making is fun, but not overly interesting. Mostly a lot of blind guessing for the sake of potentially funny outcomes, or cool rewards. I like that choosing skills has a chance to make story elements more rewarding. The point system is a pretty arbitrary way for tracking who is "winning" the game, but all it really does is makes it so the game ends in some reasonable amount of time. In the end, it doesn't offer the type of game play that I truly enjoy in modern gaming, but it was an interesting experience. I can appreciate the amount of work that went into making the huge book of potential story outcomes, though.

RATING: 6.5
Very nice production, nice components, good flavour, but the usual teeny tiny fonts that make you squint. The mechanism works well, but despite winning, I felt that the rich got richer, and conversely, it was near impossible to get out of some situations (one player was sex-changed and so could not win until somehow they got changed back - practically an impossible task). The texts generally worked very well, only a couple of incongruous matters, but mostly it was good. Difficult to decide which responses would work, but we became bolder as we went along. I found the movement quite limited, which reduced my enjoyment as I'd have liked to explore more. I never competed my first quest, whereas my friends all got through a few each. Also, the game was three hours with 4 players, and little downtime.

On balance therefore, I wouldn't buy this myself and I'd be wary of joining in a game on a club meeting, but if you like 3 hour games with a good flavour, then shell out the bucks for this one. I didn't dislike it, but I could have been more entertained.

RATING: 6
I like the educational aspect of the game including the basis for the map. I like how the game can be modified in many different ways to vary the length or complexity of the game.  I like how everyone sets their own secret goals for winning.  I haven't figured the odds, but playing the same game twice is virtually impossible.

The game is attractive and the components are very nice.  Although an expensive game, you get a lot of value for your buck.

There was a lot of down time reading--but the short paragraphs are often humerous and well done.  Since there are a thousand different paragraphs, there is very little repetitiveness.  If I enjoyed games with a lot of text, this game would have rated much, much higher with me.  

If you are a poor reader and don't enjoy reading, this game is not for you.  Middle school reading level and up shouldn't have a problem.

RATING: 7
Very fun game - more like collective "Choose your own adventure" than a regular game.  Lots of fun, lots of laughs.  It reminds me of Arkham Horror, only the text is so much better written that you can have fun with the story that unfolds, rather than just worrying about the game mechanics.

Also, there is very little player interaction, other than laughing at the silly situations everyone is getting in.  More player interaction within the game story would have made it more fun, I think.

Other than those 2 little things, this is a super fun game and I hope to get to try it again!

I need to try Above and Below or its sequel.  I have this feeling they would completely replace this game.

RATING: 8
I (as Ali Baba) ended up on the far side of the map on the first turn after having saved a village.  They erected a statue of me and a woman fell in love.  I welcomed her advances, but avoided entering cities lest she force me to settle down.  At some point I was gifted a hairless ape by a djinn, and cursed by another djinn.  On my way to trade the ape for transport to Baghdad, another player stole it.  I finally made it to Baghdad, where I tried to help an imprisoned princess and found myself in jail.   I managed to eventually talk my way past the guard - I was exceptionally good-looking, after all - and finally settled down with my wife in Baghdad, ending my turn there to win.

I'm not really sure of the point of the pre-game story/destiny point selection.  You don't seem to have much impact on whether you get story or destiny points during the game.  As such, it's hard to see what import the choice has beyond simply handicapping yourself if indeed we can expect story and destiny points to accumulate at roughly the same rate.  For this reason, we all played with 10S/10D as our choices, and I will probably continue to do this in future plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

House Rules: Separate all city encounters, deal 2 to each player at start of game and receive 1 new when completing one. Deal 2 quest cards to each player at start of game and keep 1. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
As cute as the game can try to be, gameplay itself is a crapshoot.
Players wander around a board, attempting to solve individual goals (with as limited a player interaction as that implies) while making nearly random guesses at actions to perform in encounters before they are told what happens. Players can't control their accumulation of points or even what statuses they pick up, particularly troubling as the game can grind to a total stand-still from a combination of statuses or limited acquisition of points.
Even if we assume that players can simply enjoy "the experience" of the thing, that is even hamstrung by the massive number of tables. Each turn starts with looking up how far you can move, then moving (which is either arbitrary or automatic based upon your current quest), drawing a card, cross-checking the space, adding together your modifiers, going through the book of tales, rolling the dice, picking a reaction (which is largely arbitrary based upon your quest and skills), looking it up in the reaction matrix, rolling the destiny die, flipping through the book of tales again, and finally you get a paragraph of text randomly burped at you by the book of tales and maybe the choice to use a skill if you have a relevant one. There are five minutes of bookkeeping for every two minutes of play!

(4 plays)
First Impressions: 2

RATING: 6
This is really fun even using the printed solo variant. The interesting part is you never know what is going to happen with each encounter. The more you play the game to it's end, the more powers you get, and the more things you have to mess around with. It's just plain fun to take your time and . The differences won't be enough to make it a life time game for you other than to introduce it to someone that's never played it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Awesome piece of work, but needs very relaxed conditions to be played - lots of time and comfy chairs, and maybe some wine.

After 4 plays, I'm convinced there's something in there. If you choose to play a seductress character, and choose seduction skills and seduce when you can, good things will happen to you. If you choose a seductress character with irrelevant skills and attack when you can, it won't work and bad things will happen to you. 

There's often a way out of the bad things. If you're wounded, rest and get rid of it. If you're imprisoned, you need to figure out how your character can defeat the jailer. If you're lost, become hopelessly lost and get over it. Don't keep the bad statuses around - take active steps to remove them.

The key to succeeding in this game is believing that you can make a difference, no matter how random it seems. As far as I can tell, it's an awesome design achievement.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Fun for telling stories, though it can also drag, particularly near the end. I recommend house rules to make the game go by more quickly. That said, you may have a rollicking good time, or you may have more of a "meh" experience, depending on any number of factors. 

My own house rule I've settled on is using any combination of Story and Destiny points to reach 20, i.e. you don't choose your own combination at the start, just forgo the tokens and whoever has a total of 20 points and gets back to Baghdad with a successful encounter wins the game.

Places of Power are hard to get to, and treasure cards are hard to come by. I do wish they came up more often.

Update: My group still enjoys this game and asks for it. We're just a bunch of story-readers and story-tellers, I suppose. Nobody ever minds the sheer randomness of it.

RATING: 10
Tales of the Arabian Nights is extraordinary. It's like a choose-your-own-adventure novel but with so many more options and the story is so much more enjoyable because you share it with your friends.
Of course, luck plays a MAJOR part in this game. If you have the ill fortune to become grief-stricken, that usually takes away your chancce of winning. Not being able to use your skills really sucks. If you are really unlucky, it is possible that you are effectively not participating in the game anymore because the other players get to choose where you go, who you encounter and how you react. For these reasons, I totally understand if other people don't like this game. 
To me, the experience is unparalleled and because the stories are so unique each time, I simply love this game and luckily my friends do too.

RATING: 6

You must absolutely submit to the story, because you often have very little control - to the extent that it's not really a game, more of an interactive story. But if you're in the right frame of mind there are many laughs to be had.

I like the idea of Z-Man's PvP variant - seems a good way to add a thin veil of strategy, and also gives more options for getting rid of very difficult statuses like Sex Changed. But having tried it I'm tempted to house rule even more (and more positive) opportunities for interaction between players.

RATING: 8
The RPG geek in me had a stroke when I found out this was going to be re-released.

Man, is this box ever heavy!
Some seriously quality stuff inside, with a simply massive tome for the book of tales, and a beautiful mounted game board. The box itself is practically bullet proof. Z-man really delivers on the quality vs. price for sure. The only complaint about the components I can make is that the finish on the cards is a bit thin, and looks like it's going to wear out fast. My board is also warping slightly the longer it's sat in the closet. other than these two minor gripes, the components are really, really nice.

The game looks easy enough to play, and offers a great amount of replayability. This one looks like it'll spend lots of time off the shelf, as the Lady and I both enjoy adventure games, and the fact that this one is purely story-telling is a nice change in what normally constitutes an adventure game. ie. killing monsters, levelling up, and getting treasure.

The game is a very unique, and really tells you a neat little story. I assumed there would be a lot more descriptive text in the paragraphs, and my adventure as Sinbad seemed a bit lacklustre.
That said, it's a really fun game with a lot of replayability. I just don't think it had enough suspenseful moments or action for my personal tastes. 

I also found the constant page-flipping to be a bit of a pain, and the encounter tables printed on a sheet like the matrices would have been much easier to use.

A really good classic, unique board game experience.
Get this game if you seek something different with a lot of theme and a great deal of charm.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Depending on my mood, I'll want to give this game a lower, or higher score. Right now, it's sitting at 7, but as I type this I want to change it to 6.5.
Maybe 6.
But I can't. This game has something no other game in my library can boast: REAL STORY TELLING. I bought Mice and Mystics because it was supposed to be a story-telling game.
It isn't. 
I play the card game Once Upon A Time with my friends, but those stories become goofy efforts at one-upmanship that OCCASIONALLY linger in my mind. It's one great game for every four or five plays. 
Tales of Arabian nights is Pure, Amazing, STORY!
And, not much game. 
Not much game at all. 
Oh, sure, it has a gorgeous game board, great artwork, loads of tokens, and lots of cards. LOTS OF CARDS! But the "game" element is almost non-existent. 
The basis of this experience is a mammoth book of tales that enables you to participate in a group "Choose your own adventure." One person looks up a matrix of your current stats and location and then gives you the name of an encounter (person, place or thing) that you choose to interact with based on another matrix. It gets a bit challenging, but all that cross-referencing results with someone reading you a WELL WRITTEN (looking at you Mice and Mystics) engrossing, paragraph that ends with you making further choices, or gives you options based on your stats. From that point, you might make a choice, and your narrator might read some more, and in the time it takes you to rub a magic lamp, you end up in prison, or changed into an animal, or gain some cool new trait.
It's very linear, but absolutely brilliant.

The person reading your story can add to the game immensely by using their own unique personality and verbal/acting skills as they tell you about the Sultan who dispatches henchmen to throw you into a pit for beating a beggar, or how you used a magic bow to shatter a crystal mountain. 
The stories are good, but it's all in the performance. A good reader can elevate your game experience to an evening of high art, or leave you yawning into your palm if they read it like a stodgy librarian who's too bored with their own life to bother breathing life into the printed word.

That's why I waver on the score because this game is all about who you play with. 
That, and it takes TOO long, and as you get more traits and skills, it gets harder and harder to understand the choice matrix which leads to confusion and can leave you arguing over the rules.
It can fall apart very quickly, but you have to respect what the designers have done with this game, even if it leaves you wishing they had actually included a game.

What they have done is gift you with a box of wonders, and it's up to you what you do with it.

I think that, in itself, is an excellent use of the theme.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It's hard to determine a rating for this game. On the one hand, it is engaging and fun to play, but it isn't really much of a [i]game[/i]. Starting with a 6, may drop to 5.

Players move their characters through a map and have adventures which reward them with points (Story and/or Destiny), treasure, skills, or some combination of those. The game ends when someone has reached their predicted level of Story and Destiny points.

Unfortunately, there's very little one can do to actually influence what type or how many points one receives. Also, you have minimal influence on anything that happens to other players as well. So you must listen to their stories, and on your own turn make just one choice and listen to your own story. Listening to all these tales takes a long time, and that's a problem as well.

If this game moved more quickly and gave you more direct control over your destiny, I would like it better. However, both of those things are kind of the opposite of what the game is going for. It's supposed to be a leisurely meandering storytelling session with friends, so on that level it succeeds.

I imagine that people who like this sort of game would rather be playing D&D, and those who don't would rather be playing anything else.

RATING: 7
Interesting game. The story events are very dream-like (imagistic, episodic). It was hard to string these together into a common developing narrative, and I'm not sure it's possible. The stories are a collection of events without the internal unity of development (or rather, without that special something makes experiences reasonate together in a continuous thread rather than remain segmented into atomized events). I have decided (for now) that that games without a unity of development don't have narrative, and therefore that TotAN cannot sustain a running narrative. As a result, while the game is fun to play and hang out with, it is missing one key element for compelling stories: dramatic tension.

RATING: 6
3-1-10
My comments didn't reflect a 7-worthy game, so I've lowered it to a 6.  I still feel this game is too random and feels like a toy rather than a game.  Below comments still apply.

Everything is far too random.  You randomly pick how you win, you randomly guess what actions to use on which encounters, hoping you've picked the right choice to get you the right amount of random points for your random winning condition.  You pick up random skills trying to use them at random times, and random curses and positive statuses will come your way.  The quests come out randomly for each player and everything feels like it is out of your hands.  As though you're not really playing, but just watching.

It can be good for some laughs but seems like more of a toy than a game.  With the right people I'd play it again but I'd likely prefer something else.

RATING: 7
Tales of the Arabian Nights is a Choose your own Adventure game, with lots of deliberately added chaos thrown in, all for fun.

While not a roll-&-move, there is a deliverate amount of variability in a player's ability to choose their own actions (much less destiny), and a plethora of chits and statuses needing sorted and maintained.

Furthermore, it's longish- but I like it. The Choose your own Adventure model was fun, but too limited- this takes players in hundreds of directions, and gives them a chance to role play, and just enjoy the hand of Kismet in their alter-ego's lives.

RATING: 10
[i]An extremely complicated [gameid=5432] variant.[/i]

[b]Facts about [i]Tales[/i]:[/b]

- That which does not enrich you will grant you beatings.

- You can drink your way out of a rockslide.

- Allah says NO.

:goldencamel:

[b]Optional adjustments for maximum enjoyment:

~ Change time of day at the start of each new round[/b]
Instead of waiting for the deck to run out—which [i]never[/i] happens—advance time at the beginning of the first player's turn:  morning --> day --> night --> morning --> &c.

[b]~ Consider playing to a hard total of Story plus Destiny[/b]
Rather than having each player choose a secret combination.  For example, first person to get S+D=12 can win.  (This converts a multi-hour slog into a delightful hour-plus—which means we get to play it more often!)

[b]~ Go more RPG than board game with the statuses[/b]
Some statuses can overstay their welcome and/or become unfun when combined with others—don't be afraid to hand-wave them away with a merciful "You have suffered enough."*

[b]~ Only play with awesome people[/b]
'Nuff said.

[size=7]*YMMV, but personally I love getting dogpiled under a mound of crazy statuses—in my last game I was a scorned, crippled, diseased outlaw vizier.  HELL YEAH[/size]

Upgraded with a set of these:

[imageid=3519646 small]

RATING: 8
SO many improvements on the original: a few confusing rules dropped, and better component design to reduce the amount of chits, although there's still way too much crap on the table. Love how everyone gets an encounter on every turn.

We played with 5p and set the winning condition to 10 combined story/destiny points (user-determined formula). It went a bit over 3 hours; would probably try 8 points for that many players next time.

3/5 players ended up winning, so with that kind of pacing I think it would be easy to project game length if you know the relationship between the number of rounds & points to win the game. Gotta remember to track that next time.

Fun party game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I knew what to expect going in and it didn't live up to my expectations. The stories are disconnected and totally random. Sure it generates a series of potentially interesting/amusing anecdotes but they only merge into a real narrative arc if you supply heaps of your own imagination. Which I can do better via some kind of storytelling RPG like Fiasco or Primetime Adventures or Baron Munchausen.

Several of the actual mechanics of the game fail badly: you get to choose between two different kinds of points as your final goal but none of us could ever figure out how to get one versus the other. Taking anything other than the default split seemed silly. The game has morning, afternoon, and night phases but we didn't get anywhere close to morning (after ~3 hours of play).

This is for people who want to play a storytelling RPG but don't want to try to convince family/friends.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I think most of us are familiar with the story of Scheherazade and the tales of 1001 Arabian Nights.  Given that the whole collection is about a woman using her skills as a storyteller, it's entirely appropriate that Tales of the Arabian Nights revolves around story rather than strategy.

This is entirely an "experience" game, where the primary goal is enjoy the "story" rather than win the game.  There are decisions to be made but no way to reasonably predict the impact those decisions will have.  That said, the paragraphs are well written and entertaining, all the more so for being unpredictable.  If you don't mind the random feel to decisions and the story aspect of the game appeals to you, chances are you'll really enjoy this game.  If not, I'd suggest steering clear or, at the very least, trying the game before buying it.

As for me, I'm a professional storyteller (part time).  I think the game is brilliant.

RATING: 5
A simple role-playing game that essentially consists of making a random choice of action is response to an equally random encounter. The problem is that no real world common sense can assist you, the 'barber' that you meet may turn out to be evil, the 'beggar' scorns your assistance. No matter how many skill tokens you acquire you never seem to have the right one to benefit, so the game only ramps up slowly. This may be playable for a laugh, but that's highly group dependent, gamers tend to be a logical lot, making this more family fare.
