RATING: 8
This is such a departure from the sort of game I usually look for, and also an amazing amount of fun.  It's all about the stories, and the stories are pretty good. I also like how players are free to wander around the world and do their thing; it compares favorably to games like Betrayal at the House on the Hill where eventually you all just have to go to some room and roll dice, which isn't very exciting. In this game you are always doing something interesting and new. Well, as long as you aren't in prison.

The best part? Reading the paragraphs for someone else.  That's why it seems best to play with four; you are usually either reading or playing.  

There is some ablism in the game, and, you know, I kind of wish there weren't.  But it's not enough to put me off the game.  (1 play)

First played July 16th, 2009. Taught in 15 minutes, played in 2 & 1/2 hours with 5 players. This game is more about an adventure and taking your character on that adventure. Everone is given a quest card the must complete before they make it back to Bagdad. You also must gain 15/20 points as well. Before you start, you choose a token from both Story and Destiny to total this number and keep it hidden. Everyone starts at Bagdad as well. Each person has a Story, Destiny, and Wealth track(start on Poor). The Wealth track determines how fast you move(3 on land and 2 by sea to start). Each person is allowed to pick three skills to start as well. On your turn, you can move on the board towards ful-filling your quests and then you draw an encounter card. The person on your left looks up the matrix number on the card in the book and then the person on the right looks up the matrix table for what you drew and which action you decided to pick and then the person on the left looks up the outcome. You are rewarded with skills, lose/gain story/destiny/wealth points depending on your current skills and what action you chose. You can also get city cards that you keep. If you reach that city again, you get two encounter cards. It seems to be a bit overerly figity for me. If you like stories and a group game with some light fun, this is a good game to try out.

RATING: 9
Is it technically a game? Debatable.

Is it incredibly fun? Yes. Oh, yes.

I have a feeling storytelling mode (in which players take the core aspects of a story result from the book and then apply their own spin) will truly be the best way to play the game. 

Update: Indeed, after playing a few games in which the player's took liberties with the story results in the book, and applied some of our own narrative flavor and personality, the game launched into another stratosphere. Easily one of the most fun games ever, and it's always requested that I bring it along to any game nights. I will play this anytime, anywhere, with anyone.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Tales of the Arabian Nights is less a game than a group choose-your-adventure story.  Set in the fantasy setting of Arabian Nights, you each choose a character along with two starting skills.  You then each take a turn moving your piece on the board.  You each have a mission card that can provide some direction to your travels but basically you just want to go to happening spots (with a high number).  There you pick a card to discover what happens and roll a die to find out how big an encounter it was.  You cross-reference a table and then your fellow player tells you what sort of character or event you have encountered (e.g., a hungry tiger or an ugly barber).  You then make a choice, consisting of a single verb from a table of relevant actions (e.g., "drink", "fight", etc).  The fellow player then looks up the corresponding paragraph number and rolls a -/+/0 die roll and then reads aloud to you a brief story about your encounter, full of djinn, evil viziers, monsters, and treasure.   The relationship between your choice and the resulting story is often a bit mysterious.  Oftentimes if you have a particular skill (e.g., "magic", "faith", "storytelling") then you'll have a good ending and some kind of payoff, including more skills or the Story and Fate points you need to win.  You can try choosing actions that steer the stories towards your skills but that can get boring.  If you have an advanced version of the skill (by getting it twice) then the reader will skip the final die roll and simply choose the story that allows your master skill to be used if such an option is present.  The multiple steps can get a little clumsy but the stories are quite charming and fun.  Needless to say, there is a huge luck factor and a runaway winner syndrome as accumulating skills result in yet better outcomes.  It's best to approach it as more of a party game and just enjoy the ride.  Taken in this way, I definitely enjoy playing it from time to time with the right group.  I do recommend house ruling the status "grief" to be just until the next good thing happens to you as otherwise its effect (to not have any skills) is crippling and, worse, leads to boring stories.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Nothing that hasn't already been said about this.  There's not a lot of "game" here.  But it's a great light role playing experience, and the production values are fantastic.

Initial Rating: 7.0 (February 2012)

I didn’t think I would like this game but I do. You don’t have a lot of deep, meaningful decisions to make in tales of Arabian Nights, however the game is too fun to pass and I really enjoy myself when I play it :D. In this game you can´t plan your moves, you have to let yourself go and flow with the story.

I think this is a 3 player game, more than that and the downtime can be excessive (as you don’t have anything to think/do until it is your turn). This is the first game I really wish had an Spanish version, last time we played I got throat ache because I had to read and translate everything.

Good game, I want to play it again, just not every time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Based on the previews and rules, I was optimistic that Z-Man had done an excellent job with this reprint.  But that couldn't compare to actually experiencing the redone Tales.  In almost all ways this printing improves on the West End version, and it will undoubtedly become the definitive version of the game.  Statuses are now cards, eliminating the irritating need to look up a paragraph for each status a character has.  City cards have been fixed so that they lead to an encounter, and are uniformly positive thus giving players an incentive to actually use them.  "Take that" cards are gone, mercifully, though certain statuses allow one to bestow their effects on other players.  Quests are a fun new addition.  The art is fantastic, and the new board integrates the fiddly "cradle of civilization" encounter modifiers and loses the fiddliness.  The story paragraphs are detailed enough not to force any embellishment on the reader's part, but open-ended enough to allow for some if the reader is a talented storyteller.  Also there are more than twice the number of paragraphs as in the old Book of Tales, and the reaction matrices have been updated to eliminate stupidity like the "infinite hunchback loop" from the old game.  Skills have been cleaned up so there's no longer that silly distinction between Minor and Major magic, and Bargaining and Evaluation are combined into one skill that actually doesn't suck.

I'm still not sold on the story/destiny goal, which seems to reduce the game to a collection of victory points, but I'm not sure how to replace it.  Possibly with a time limit or a set number of turns.  Once in a while a group should play to Sultan for the epic experience, but one thing I noticed when playing a Sultan variant is that the players tend to figure out what roads lead to Sultan.  That's bad.

For what I think about the original game, see my comment on the West End version.  To sum up, it's a fantastic roleplaying experience that only vaguely fits the definition of "game."  As long as all players know that going in, that isn't a problem.

I agree with those who play only with 4 or less - I would even say the optimal numbers are 2 and 3.  Watching the action is fun, but it's much more fun participating and the downtime really would get intolerable with 5 or above.  2-player, 2 characters each is probably also viable.  It was viable with the West End version at least.

RATING: 4
This isn't a game.

There really is no decision making outside of what space you'd like to move to and how you'd like to engage the encounter. Once that's done another player will read a little story to you, ask if you have an applicable skill then read you the outcome which is most often ridiculous.

Now I admit that I did get a few laughs out of the stories but there was really nothing that I did with my character that made them better or worse. In several cases I was directed in how I was to respond to an encounter by a trait I had, or worse yet, someone else deciding for me.

If you've enjoyed playing any RPG you may want to give this a try just to see what it's like. My guess is like me you'll suggest playing the RPG instead of this.

If you haven't played an RPG and enjoy this then you should really look into playing an RPG to experience what a good story adventure game can be.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game goes totally against the grain of the types of games I rate highly, and yet... Although I had not played this prior to 2011, it sure takes me back to my days of reading "Chose Your Own Adventure" books and the old "Alone on Halloween" solo Cthulhu adventure, where I used to narrate the story to a friend as he adventured and then he drew pictures of the various grisly ways in which he died. Ah, good times!

I know some contend this isn't even a game, but I disagree with that. You do make choices throughout, even if the outcome of almost all your choices is uncertain. The point here, though, is to have a story unfold, with your choices as the primary driver of variability and destiny, and it works on that level. I think what impressed me most is how the concept of "leveling up" manifests itself in the form of greater control. While the outcome of your choices is heavily influenced by luck, the destiny and story points and skills you obtain primarily give you a greater ability to manipulate outcomes, bringing the whims of fate more under control (although never completely). The game works so well in fact, I have to tip my hat to the designer. Being a former RPG'er I enjoy a game with a good story just as much as a tense, mind-bending economic exercise. Sadly, most story games (games with a developing character arc) become victims of their own labyrinthine mechanics (Magic Realm) or reduce the story aspect to a monotonous loop of fight, get loot, fight, get loot, and so on (too many to pick just one...). Arabian Nights, though, maintains huge variability and successfully adapts a solo adventure framework into an exceptional multi-player experience. I can't wait until my kids are old enough for this!

The big knock for me with this game is length. It can outstay its welcome, especially if everyone isn't totally into the stories unfolding.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

This review is after one play only; I have never quite played a game like this, (although it sort of reminded me of my D&D games as a kid), but I really liked what I experienced. Love the Arabian Nights, and I really like the beautiful pieces. I think the key to enjoying this game is playing with the right people. The other 2 people I played with don't like cut-throat strategy games where everyone is out to win, so this game was right up their alley. At first, while reading the rules, I really did not like the homophobia present in terms of courting (and no, I don't want to get into that. Already been done on the boards), but any house rule can override such silly rules. I'm wondering what play will be like with a larger group.

RATING: 8
This game is more like a choose your own adventure book than a game. There is some game there - there are points and a winner, but it is best to enter the game with an attitude of, "let's see what adventure unfolds." On the down side, the adventure can be frustrating at times (you might end up stuck for several turns) and it can run too long, but over all this is a very enjoyable time. Also, some of the story elements are so general that they can be nonsensical, but that can be amusing, too. RPG'ers or other storytellers can paraphrase the story line to make it more relevant and interesting, but you don't need this skill for the game to be fun. As in many games and game nights, the right attitude is the key.
Relax with a glass or two of your favorite beverage, some snacks, and share the adventure with your family and friends.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Warning: not a competitive game (at all). See my comment on the original West End Games edition for more detail. 

I've now played the new edition once (7/22/09). I've played the original West End Edition around 15 times, and it's one of my favorite games. This edition certainly looks better (although the original can stand on its own merits), but the functionality has been greatly improved. Statuses are in card form instead of on counters, so the effects are right in front of you and do not need to be constantly double-checked in the book of tales. The city cards have been fixed, although they were so sloppy in the original it's hard to imagine anyone opting not to house rule them. Quests have been added. The DP/SP ramp ups have been tweaked and now trigger somewhat sooner. Bargaining and Evaluation are now one skill. Major and Minor magic are now one skill. The WEG Book of Tales has 1400 paragraphs. The Z-man edition has 2600 and comes in a very durable spiral bound book (several plays would reduce the WEG book to tatters). 

A great edition of a great game. 

Thanks to Z-man for reprinting one of the most entertaining games ever. 

**My one caveat with this game is that it could better match outcomes to decisions, or narrative to role-playing. However it's tough to feel strongly about this when Tales does it better than every other game on the market. Face it; designers suck at this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I can see why this game was so highly sought after while it was out of print.  It certainly is unique, especially for those (like me) that have never played an RPG.  I'm assuming that this is not really an RPG, but it my mind I imagine them to be somewhat similar.  I guess that's all conjecture, so I think of it more as a choose your own adventure type game.

You definitely have to have the right kind of group for a game like this.  Since it's not really a competitive event, but rather mainly solitaire, getting into the spirit of the game, especially while reading from the book, is essential.  It also needs to keep moving along quickly, since there's not really anything for you to do during someone else's turn other than listen and enjoy.

The work that went into organizing the game is tremendous and is truly impressive.  It's really cool how the matrices work and it's fun to consider your options.  An imprisoned princess?  Hmm...would it better to aid or attack her?  A shiny artifact - take it or enter it?

The only thing I wish it that there was some easier way to get to where you need to go in the book.  This type of game is just screaming for an iPhone/iPad app that will quickly tell you where to turn.  I don't mind turning to the actual paragraph, but having to look at the two different matrices is a little annoying.

RATING: 7
Compared to previous editions the third one discards the flawed merchant rules entirely; dispenses with the storytelling and solo rules, although they are available as downloadable texts adapted with minimal changes from the second edition; exchanges the largely themeless quest rules for a new card-based system that is part of the standard game; changes the city cards so that they provide encounters and also have no outright bad results; and adds a considerable amount of new paragraphs. There are a few other improvements that could have been made and ambiguities that could have been resolved, and the city cards may be a tad generous, but all things considered this is the version most people should be playing.

RATING: 7
Excellent story telling game.  Not for the gamer who demands perfectly balanced games with firms rules on how to win. This game relies on chance and it's not really "played to win". Rather, sit back and enjoy the story that unfolds.

I HIGHLY recommend using house rules to lessen the negative effects or at least lessen the duration of the effects. Download the home rules and player mats in the Files section here at BGG. Otherwise, this game can be brutal and frustrating at times.

RATING: 7
What can i say about this one?
I don't think it is really a game like anything I've played before. It comes closest to a dungeon crawler.
In the game you will have many encounters and depending on which action you take you are given a situation. In that situation yau may use one of your skills  or even have to use one of your skills. After that encounter you get some  story points and destination points, perhaps some nw skills, treasures and some statusses.

Basicly the game is build upon the situations you can encounter (2600) a lot with different options.
You should know by now if this is something you want to play or not. the game was won by someone who likes to carefully plan . And actually yhat's pretty strange. As a lot of these encounters just happen to you. He just got lucky allthough he will certainly disagree and after the game he explained that a good skill set was important.
Probably forgetting that he acquired the skillset by the lucky draw of encounters.

He liked it,  the other friend I played it with didn't.
I wouldn't mind playing it again with someone who likes it. Actually it's light on the brain to play. so it would make the perfect game in between two heavy ones or end a gameday. allthough it takes rather long to play
The downside to it is that the game is heavily language dependant (you read stories) and the english isn't exactly the most easy ones (you read stories with a lot of flavour and mysterious encounters)

Would I recommend it?
that's a tough one and you really have to decide that for yourself. 
I would stay away from it, if you can't find it in your language and you don't have a big friendgroup that can play it in another language.
I also would stay away from it if you want to play this seriously. It's a game you just have to play for fun.
You have some say in your destiny but you can also go insane and then you don't have a say, or be grieve stricken

I do think it is the perfect game for 3 or 4 players.
3 because everyone is involved. Player , storyteller and matrixreader.
4 because someone can get some chips or drinks when he/she has nothing to do (you can listen to the crazy stories developing though)

RATING: 7
In Tales of the Arabian Nights, you are the hero or heroine in a story of adventure and wonder just like those told by Scheherazade to her spellbound sultan! You will travel the land seeking your own destiny and fortune. You will learn stories and gain wisdom to share with others. Will you be the first to fulfill your destiny? The next Tale is yours to tell! There is, of course, a winner in Tales of the Arabian Nights, but the point of the game is less to see who wins and more to enjoy the unfolding and telling of a great story!

RATING: 9
► Best (passive) storytelling game.


[BGCOLOR=#339900][COLOR=#FFFFFF]▲[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] One of the best-looking games available.

[BGCOLOR=#339900][COLOR=#FFFFFF]▲[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] High production quality.

[BGCOLOR=#339900][COLOR=#FFFFFF]▲[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] Easy rules.

[BGCOLOR=#339900][COLOR=#FFFFFF]▲[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] Unique concept.

[BGCOLOR=#339900][COLOR=#FFFFFF]▲[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] Very high replay value: 300 pages for the Book of Tales (featuring ~2500 unique encounters)

[BGCOLOR=#339900][COLOR=#FFFFFF]▲[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] Humour.

[BGCOLOR=#339900][COLOR=#FFFFFF]▲[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] Freely adjustable playtime length.


[BGCOLOR=#FF0000][COLOR=#FFFFFF]▼[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] Could be described as being less of a "game" and more of an "experience".

[BGCOLOR=#FF0000][COLOR=#FFFFFF]▼[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] Highly random gameplay.

[BGCOLOR=#FF0000][COLOR=#FFFFFF]▼[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] 1 very minor house rule is necessary to unlock the game's full potential (see below).


Because of its very strong focus on storytelling and overall high randomness, some people would probably not consider [b]Tales of the Arabian Nights[/b] to be an actual "game". Maybe they are right, but it is to me still without any doubt an achievement in board gaming: absolutely fantastic components (with a certain unique vintage charme), high replay value (thanks to the gigantic Book of Tales), very easy rules, many and at the same time very different choices on how to react to the game and - last but not least - often absolutely hilarious humour.

Don't play this game if you are a competitive player or someone who needs to have full control at all times in a board game. Instead, gather your friends, hand out preferred beverages and enjoy the ride, as choosing and seeing your reaction play out to an event often feels like getting your wish granted by some mischievous Djinn bound into the Book of Tales, meaning that the outcome of your reaction often can play out quite differently than first expected.

I would like to rank this game a full 10/10, but it's objectively just too random and light for that, plus downtime with more than 3 or 4 players can be a problem.

A truly unique gem, and I absolutely love it for what it is and offers.

[hr]
[b]Essential house rule:[/b] change the daytime [i][b]every time[/b][/i] the first player begins his/her turn again. Otherwise, you will be stuck in the morning phase throughout the [i][b]entire[/b][/i] game and won't be able to reach and experience many of the book's encounters, as running through the whole encounter deck (which normally triggers the change of daytime by the official rules) literally [b][u]never[/u][/b] happened in my many games with 2-4 players.

RATING: 8
Definitely an enjoyable, story telling romp through the stories of Arabian nights!  Reminds me quite a bit of the old choose your own adventure books except in the game you likely will have skills that will at times help or hurt you in how you respond to situations.  As other people also have written, this isn't really a game you play to win, it is more a game you play to experience and enjoy the story.  Winning is just another reward on top of the experience.

I do take issue with the complaint some people have that you have very little decisions to make in this game.  In my six plays thus far, quite often taking into account which skills you have will play a significant role in the outcome of an encounter.  Sure, there will be those times where blind luck comes in for potentially the whole game (or unluckiness), but not as often as people sometimes say.

I would recommend only playing with 3; perhaps 4 at most.  Consider also just playing with a variant where instead of deciding on your victory condition before the game - just make it that your two scores have to = 20 and the minimum of each category is 5.

I do wish Z-man would have made a separate printout for the matrices.  I dislike flipping through the book twice for an encounter.  Ah well, maybe I will make a copy of that section so I can have my separate printout...:-)

RATING: 1
This is not a good game. I've played twice and both times the game seemed to delight in making me suffer. The first game I played I ended up in prison and literally was unable to get out no matter what I tried to do. Because of this I was basically unable to play the game. Combine meaningless turns with a long time to get around the table for me to try again made for a poor experience.

The second time went better, but turns still took forever and it didn't seem to matter how well I played the role I chose at the beginning, the results of my actions were almost always bad, and sometimes very random, for instance I went into a barbershop and got hit by a cart on the way out leaving me crippled.

In neither game I played did I feel like I was playing against the other players, I felt like I was playing against the game. That can be great for cooperative games, but this isn't meant to be cooperative. It may feel better to just play by myself if I want to enjoy a fun story.

The game elements are trivial, you choose skills and choose where to go and roll a die. Most of the time I felt like my skills were meaningless, I tried playing a warrior and fighting my way out of things, but it always ended badly. Choosing where to go is almost meaningless and sometimes results in situations that make no sense, for instance in one game we had a whirlpool on a mountain top. And rolling is just to make things a little less predictable. Often the situations have outcomes that are based on information you weren't given as a "player." For instance I saw a jewel in a tree, went to take it, only to discover that the jewel wasn't actually in a tree, but was hanging around the neck of a heavily guarded woman under a tree.

If you don't care about playing an actual game and just want to enjoy the storytelling, this will be great for you. But don't expect it to feel like a fun game because it's not.

RATING: 8
[u]Comments:[/u]
I get it. It's not really a game, it's more an exercise in story telling. I went in with high hopes for this one because I thought it sounded like a pretty interesting concept. After playing all I can ask myself is "why didn't I just play an RPG?" At least in an RPG I would feel like my choices had some bearing on the outcome of what was happening to me. It is an exercise in story telling, but it's not like you are writing the story yourself. It's more like a story reading exercise. The events happen and you are reading them out loud, but that's it. Encounters are completely random.
the components are quite good in this version though, and everything works pretty well together. I just don't understand the draw. Maybe it isn't for me. I would like to play it again, and see if this is really how the game is, or if it was just my imagination.  I would like to play a 3 player game instead of the longish 5 player game though.

[u]Comments Update:[/u]
After thinking about this one for a few weeks, and wanted to try it again I finally got a chance to. We went into this one taking out the "winning" conditions for the game, and just playing for a set amount of time. This made the game WAY more enjoyable to me, and allowed players to discard any min-maxing that they would have done had it been a game (in the competitive sense).  Instead we just did the craziest things we could think of to see where the story took our characters. Find a mystical fire? Why don't I just drink the mystical fire? Run into a Wicked Enchantress? Well of course you should rob her. In the end I think this makes for more enjoyable TotAN experience. The story grows, and you see where it takes you. You laugh at your own, and other player's, misfortune.  And of course getting to one of the magical cities makes each game worth it, as you sit riveted to what could possibly happen to the character. 
I certainly don't want to play this all the time, but once every two months, or so, would make me happy. I think I might even have to pick this one up for myself.

RATING: 5
The game premise is great: A choose-your-own adventure romp on the Arabian Nights world. It can be fun to play once, but it's deeply flawed.

You only have 2 decisions to make every turn:
1. Where to go.
2. Which action to choose in response to the encounter.

The second one is basically random. Most of the time the outcome will be nothing like what you expected, so you might as well roll a die and let it pick for you.

The first one will matter if you're trying to reach a quest or city location. Other than that, it's pretty meaningless.

However, the ultimate game design sin is that there are plenty of statuses that will take away one of those decisions (ensorcelled, insane, etc).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A masterpiece of random crap happening to you. Definitely more of a shared story-telling activity than a game. Don't buy if you don't like that. Can be exhausting after a while. There are also some serious problems that need to be house-ruled:

- The target score of 20 is too high for player counts greater than 2. The game drags on absolutely forever. I recommend even going as low as 10-12 in a three player game.

- Some statuses are brutally hard to get rid of and ruin a new player's fun. (Lost, Grief Stricken, etc.) I sometimes house-rule these to expire after a few turns.

- If you switch to noon when you run out the entire encounter deck as the rules prescribe, you will never get to noon. I don't know what they were thinking, I have never run it out even in a four player game to 20 points. I recommend switching the day/night cycle every round, or shrinking the encounter deck if you want to experience the noon and night encounters.

- No player interaction! This is a story-telling game, we need more things for players to do together! There are only a couple statuses you can lose or gain by interacting with another player. (Giving another player "Grief Stricken" using Storytelling is *hilarious*, we need more of that please!)

- Many situations do not involve the skill you would expect. I can roll with the disappointment, but it does seem egregious after a while, since the non-skilled result is usually "nothing happens" or "something awful happens". I sometimes make up my own skill checks if a player has something that seems like it should be relevant, but the book of tales should provide more options. (Wtf is the difference between "seduction", "beguiling", and "appearance"?)

- The encounters are capricious and brutal. Some players will be fabulously wealthy with 20 magical artifacts, while others are just lost for 10 turns and lose all their skills. Sometimes you can get screwed for *having* a skill.

RATING: 6
This is the hands down best Choose Your Own Adventure Game to date.  For what this game is there is none better.  If you go into this expecting some sort of strategy game you will be very disappointed.  If you go into this with the expectations of having a great time with friends telling stories and enjoying the way the tales unfold you can do no batter.

Your choices are relevant in this game regardless of what some think.  The 3 skills you choose in the beginning of the game should be the way your character approaches the choices.  So if you have the Stealth and Stealing skill you should pick Rob and NOT attack.  You have a better chance of seeing your skill come up if you play your character right.  Now this doesn't apply 100% of the time but it sure does apply a lot. Choosing to attack someone without the weapon use skill is also a dumb move.  Same with Insult.  

Just know what you are going to be playing before you sit down to the table.  This is NOT a game for everyone and just wanting to try it when you know for a fact that the randomness and status cards could make you hate the game why even bother.  The game take 90 minutes with 3 players and I do not recommend any more then that play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A story telling game based on the stories in Tales of Arabian Nights.  You move around a board encountering a character, location or item.  You then consult a table and roll a dice to see what specifically you encounter.  You then have a series of options that you can choose from in a "choose your own adventure format".  This points you to a location where you read aloud what happens to you and then you may benefit from abilities or make decisions that impact the outcome.  

The game is pretty light and it seems the more outrageous way you deal with an encounter, the better you seem to perform. 

I like the game, but you have to enjoy the story element to the game.  There is not much strategy.  

Be warned that for kids under 12, there are some elements of Tales of Arabian Nights (slavery, violence, indirect sexual references, etc) that may be inappropriate for younger kids.

In this new edition of the groundbreaking storytelling game, you enter the lands of the Arabian Nights alongside Sindbad, Ali Baba, and the other legendary heroes of the tales. Travel the world encountering imprisoned princesses, powerful 'efreets, evil viziers, and such marvels as the Magnetic Mountain and the fabled Elephant's Graveyard.
Choose your actions carefully and the skills you possess will reward you: become beloved, wealthy, mighty - even become sultan of a great land. Choose foolishly, however, and become a beggar, or be cursed with a beast's form or become insane from terror! YOU will bring to life the stories of the inestimable Book of Tales in this vastly replayable board game with over 2002 tales that will challenge, amuse, astound and spellbind you for years to come.

RATING: 6
The new Z-Man edition is clearly a labour of love, but on the whole, this has not aged too well, I’m afraid. While the idea of a collaborative storytelling tool is very appealing and the Decision Matrix coupled to the three results per decision point is a brilliant approach to ensure somewhat dynamic pathways, the lack of consistency that plagues many entries lets it down for me.

As outrageous or unlikely as many of the original 1001 Tales of the Arabian Nights might be, even the voice of Sheherazade herself could not have saved a story where Sindbad happens to discover a secret cave on a ship. His own ship. Such clunkiness prevents all immersion, and without immersion, there’s just not a lot of game here. Consequently, this is an interesting artifact of gaming history, but everything it does has been done better by computer games or tabletop RPGs.

RATING: 6
Light on gameplay and heavy on narrative, this game is a pure story-teller. There's really no decision making other than where to go, but the game's fun is to be had in the story that each game tells. This is NOT a game for anyone looking for strategy, but it is most definitely makes for an entertaining game night.

RATING: 9
When I was a kid, I couldn't get enough of choose-your-own-adventure books. They provided a simplified RPG experience with more storytelling and less bookkeeping.

Tales of the Arabian Nights does exactly that in a board game, each player taking turns encountering all sorts of fascinating scenarios, attempting to use the skills they've learned to overcome obstacles. What ends up happening is often out of your control and frequently hilarious. The game tells a story about your character that can sometimes be steered in your favor, and other times not, but it is always an entertaining experience that is fun to share with others.

RATING: 8
Seems fiddly at first, but the "gameplay" is really simple.
Roll dice, other player reads you stuff, next turn.

The "game" is only about the stories and i have really enjoyed every story so far. 
Maybe the story doesn't unfold as a story per se, because it doesn't have a beginning, middle and an ending, it is only the middle part all the time, until the game just ends because someone reached the necessary points.

Most of the time i rather play a game over Arabian Nights, but every now and then i feel like reading some funny stories and for that Arabian Nights has delivered.

I rate it this high, because it offers something completely different, it has a lot of variation in stories, items and statuses and it has generated a lot of laughs in a year already.

If you want a game that is about the trip more than the destination, you want a game that doesn't involve competition and you like to read stories, you can't go wrong with this one.

EDIT: 1.7.2016 the game has legs. We still play it every now and then and the stories feel fresh and funny each and every time. Can't recommend it enough really.

RATING: 6
As many have said, this is more of an experience than a game, and I have to agree.  Unfortunately, in both of my plays (with 4 and 6 players), each one outlasted its welcome after playing for several hours.  There was a noticeable amount of downtime between turns, especially with 6 players, and we were all getting distracted and bored by the end.  I can only assume it would be considerably better with fewer players, and perhaps ideal with just 3.  There's decisions to be made every turn, but they have little strategic importance; you're basically just running around world, interacting with it randomly.  While that might sound like a negative thing, there's a ton of story to this game, and it really is a great role-playing opportunity.  With the right crowd and less people, I can picture this game being a great one to pull out for a fun change of pace.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Game in a Nutshell: A choose-your-own-adventure book style game where players explore many strange parts of the world and encounter unpredictable events that never seem to turn out the way you thought they might.

Played as: 3,3 (+1 reader),4 player

Pros: A completely unique game that creates a story like no other board game has ever come close to doing. The shear mass of possibilities is staggering and each one is so well written. Even though there’s a lack of control in the game, it’s still fun no matter where you end up. I’ve spent half the game in a jail cell and had a blast trying fruitlessly to escape (which I never did). Die? Done that, it was a riot! On a separate note, the game is beautifully presented, art and component-quality wise.

Cons: Definitely runs on the long side, and as such, anything over 3 players can quickly become tiresome. Even at that, I think I’ll play with a shortened total of required points to end the game quicker. Speaking of endgame, it’s very anticlimactic. It would have made a big difference if they’d written in some ‘and so he lived happily ever after’ or ‘and wasted away the rest of his years locked in a padded cell’ type conclusion tree that would punctuate the story line. As it is, the game never feels finalized, and you just start putting it away.  And finally, flipping through the book becomes the biggest slowdown of the game. Nothing probably can be done about that, but it still sucks.

Other: I’ve watched two groups of people immediately buy this after playing it the first time. It is possible to see some of the same storylines after only a couple plays as people instinctively always want to ‘attack’ the ‘warfleet’ rather then try approaching the encounter in a new way. So if there’s one word of advice, it’s to never hold back from trying something different.

Best Use: Turn off the TV and try creating your own storyline.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
One of our favorite games. There isn't really all that much game to it, except maybe trying to get to locations that match your skills. However, it often creates really nice stories and who doesn't want to be vizier or sultan?

We only play this with two. There isn't much interaction and one play with three already had too much downtime.

I like to set myself a certain character scheme when I start. I will pick skills for that and try to choose my actions accordingly, even if in a game sense they seem stupid. This works quite well for me.

My wife tends to rack up the story points super fast. This means that she never suffers from Grief Stricken(?) all that much, whereas it has been crippling for me.

I would encourage to instantly house-rule silly situations like endless suffering from stuff like being Grief Stricken or being Lost. Do check the rules for those things and maybe FAQ, cause the rules can be misleading. E.g. I think you can still walk while being lost.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I love games (like Android and Arkham Horror) whose function is to build a narrative. Seems like this might be my kind of thing. 

Picked up a copy from Amazon using a Gift Cert. for Birthday 2010.

============================

Thoughts after a first (solo) play. Strangely immersive. I found myself stumbling a bit through the charts and process of looking everything up - at least at first - but the story itself that it seemed to build was spookily NOT random - at least not in the way I expected. Sure, strangely random events occurred, but as I made my choices and read the results of my decisions, there was a thematic continuity to all of it - not a continuity of character or setting mind you - a THEMATIC continuity. In front of my eyes, a story of a young man seeking wisdom and truth unfolded, one where as he learned to rely on his piety and simple faith, he would not only be able to weather storms or overcome challenges, but even inspire others and gain great wealth because of it. The game seemed to be telling me: have faith. The world is a strange and terrible place - one you cannot predict or control, but the pious man, the man of wisdom and faith, is patient, prays to God, and always finds an answer.

It was touching, and beautiful, and I was almost inspired to sit down and write it all out. And then I remembered it already had been. It was sitting on my shelf over there. The Richard Burton edition.

So for what it's worth, the game is a literary treasure.

-----

Right now, relative to Android, which is the only other game I've come across that seems to want to build and develop narrative to such an extent, TotAN does feel a bit more like a wacky choose your own adventure. So while there was a thematic consistency, there was also this lack of feeling of control. It was a roller coaster, and I was along for the ride, rather than an agent of my own destiny. In Android, there is a similar sense of thematic consistency, but players still seem to be able to exert control over the narrative - at least more so than with TotAN. Still, I suspect that I will be a million times more likely to get my wife and extended family to play TotAN than Android, and perhaps that makes it the better game?

RATING: 5.2
Really?  All the fuss and foaming for this?  I was harshing on Arkham Horror for its lack of decisions and tactical choices, but AH is a deep strategy game next to AN.  Of course, AN apparently is all about storytelling, but the stories are disjointed and make no sense.  My character is traveling overland and gets sucked into an ocean whirlpool? RATING: 9.1
I think players will either love this game or hate it.... with nothing in between. 
The game is very heavily themed and if you play if for what it actually is: AN EXPERIENCE and not an out & out strategic board game, then you will thoroughly enjoy seeing your character go through the trials and tribulations as they travel the Arabian Nights world. The encounters are very well scripted and always provide laughs from the other players. It plays very much like a fighting fantasy book style adventure and works well with 3 players (one taking the actions, one looking up the encounter charts and one reading passages from the encounter script book). With more players, it could suffer from immense donwtime problems whilst people wait for other players to take their turn, and rushing things by skip reading the text will remove the theme - something to be avoided.
So there it is: for 3 players looking for an expereince it is HIGHLY RECOMMENDED. For more players, then they need to be patient, and for people looking for a strategic board game - look elsewhere.

RATING: 7.9
I have very mixed feelings about this one.
 I love it for the interesting stories, relaxed game play, and the fun we always get out of playing. I hate it for all the missed opportunities as to what it COULD have been. 
Wish there were an equal ratio of male/female playable characters and that the movement on the board correlated better with the story you receive. Wish the choices we make had some logical connection to the outcomes we experienced. Why are the goals semi-irrelevant? Etc. etc. There are so many elements that could have been executed better. 
And yet, this is still one of the best examples of this genre. We will continue to play...but not that often.

RATING: 4
Tales Of The Arabian Nights is a relic from an age before the advent of the role-playing video game.

TotAN has so little player interaction that it is essentially a solitaire game. Other players involvement is relegated to operating the machine that spits out a result from the players once-per-turn decision. It does provide a limited role-playing experience, while doing away with a D&D-style RPG need for an imaginative Dungeon Master, but severely limits players' ability to affect the outcome of the game.

TotAN should have been converted into a point-and-click adventure RPG back in the 90's where all the fiddly work is done behind the screen by the programming, leaving the player to be immersed in the narrative.

Z-man's updated version has lovely artwork, but is the equivalent of a nicely painted telegraph machine. It might be pretty, but is obsolete to the level of pointlessness.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This game is just a blast, but one that's not without some problems. There is a kind of fatigue/weariness that can set in. When it's not your turn you just can't wait for it be your turn (thus I recommend playing max 3 players). It's so easy (and kind of random) to get some really, really sucky statuses such as Ensorcelled, Scorned, or Grief Stricken, which can really make the entire game experience kind of a bad one. And unlike most popular modern games, there is no good way to estimate the length of time the game will take. But there's something wondrous about playing it, the storytelling, trying to intuit how best to react to a particular encounter based upon your available skills. E.g., I have piety, so I'll pray when I encounter the jealous wizard. Or throw caution to the wind, see what happens when you drink the house fire.
----
upping from 9. It's Candyland for adults (in the best way possible)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I'm loathe to call this a game.  It's more like what they'd call in the Sixties a "happening".  If you go into this "game" thinking you're going to have a winner, you've just lost.  If you go into it thinking you're going to have a *story*, you win.

This "game" is all about creating a story for your character and the weird things that happen to you in your life -- the points system and the "first person home wins" thing is just a MacGuffin.  And it's amusing, but SO MUCH DEPENDS on playing this game with the right people -- with creative folks willing to put in the time to talk between the dots, this game is as good as Munchausen.  Without them, you're playing Agricola without all that wonderful storytelling of the Agricola experience.

Another Book of Tales would help.  And this game has more errata than any I've seen.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.95
OMG! This is an outrageously fun game! I would say that if you enjoy Betrayal at House on the Hill with its amazing book of haunts, and if you enjoy Arkham Horror for it's endless encounters, then you will enjoy this! The theme is all 1001 Tales of the Arabian Nights and the stories are incredibly addictive and will leave you immersed! Your character will simply journey around the board, but every turn brings you a new encounter and new story! As we are discovering, ZMan games do not disappoint! The huge book of stories and beautiful components are outstanding! 

The game isn't perfect, in some rare cases it can play for a long time with no end seeming likely. For example, my wife and I played a game where we were both sex changed and could not win the game unless we changed back! Never happened...game ended in a draw after 4 hours of game play! Still, we didn't complain...too much fun!

We have found that adjusting the point totals for your gaming needs works really well. For example, in a 6 player game...especially with new players...the game can go really long. So setting the points around 3 story and 3 destiny works quite well. Also, if you are short on time, setting the points around 5 and 5 also works really well. Ultimately though, when we play with 2 to 4 experienced players and we have no limit on time, then we set the points high and have one thrilling journey.

At almost 30 plays of this game...we still haven't made it to all of the places of power. High replay ability!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
規則:
*遭遇卡洗勻放一旁，旁邊放早晨標記(每次遭遇卡用完重洗就分別改放中午/夜晚)，寶物卡洗勻放一旁，狀態卡分類放好，技能分類放好
*每個玩家選擇角色拿該顏色所有標記，角色放巴格達，命運/故事標記方在圖版0的位置，財富放貧窮格，任務卡每人一張，每人自由決定命運故事要幾點，放在個人圖版下(總和20)，擲骰點高的當首家，輪流選技能，直到每人3個，放天賦面
*遊戲輪流進行直到有玩家達到命運/故事點並回到巴格達就勝利了，每輪行動如下
1.傳遞故事書 左邊玩家拿故事書右邊拿反應矩陣
2.查看寶物跟狀態卡 宣讀效果
3.根據財富等級決定移動幾步，總步數為大的，陸地或水路移動點數也不能超過，可不動
*秘境為遊戲中遭遇發現入口時可放目的地指示物，此時才能進入，完成秘境遭遇後馬上移出，秘境遭遇直接看對應段落
*遭遇卡分三種
A 角色 根據目前時間跳到對應頁數並念
B 地形 根據目前地形念對應段落，如果是N則念反應方陣
C 城市 先根據卡片下方數字念對應段落，此卡可保留或棄掉，如果保留且抵達該城市可打出並擲骰決定獎勵
*解決遭遇時會先
A.遇到遭遇表，擲骰加角色位置數字在加命運點數旁數字(超過12等於12)念對應條目的內容
B.根據條目後英文字母查反應方陣，念出所有選項，根據選項查對應段落
C.擲骰決定要念哪一頁(可能加減一)
D.朗讀結果 根據結果拿獎勵或懲罰，如果是技能就拿，有同樣技能就升級到精通，朗讀時先念最上面，在給玩家選擇要不要使用技能，強制技能則強迫使用
*如果有精通技能且相關的三頁有對應的技能區，則可不擲骰直接念該頁(強制的不能先講)，失去精通技能則是降到天賦面
4.抽遭遇卡(除非到秘境)結算
5.在城市可打出對應城市的遭遇卡或看是否滿足任務拿獎勵並抽新的任務卡
6.確認是否勝利，其他玩家會進行最後一回合
*財富到富比王侯後，需要有至少一寶物，才會在獲得財富時到富可敵國，在城市時可以賣寶物增加左上方值，增加等級不能超過寶物下方標示
*角色死亡則回到巴格達，財富回貧窮，除技能故事命運點外都棄掉
*平手比狀態，在比技能
***變體 減少時間***
可降低20點上線，限制狀態只會有一個，一次獲得兩個時只會拿後面的，有新的就棄掉舊的
***變體 單人遊戲***
勝利條件 滿足下列三項：
1) 滿足起始選的命運 故事點
2) 富裕值必須到達富可敵國
3) 至少完成兩項任務
*若想要增加難度可以增加總點數或者以夜晚起始的
*如果在遊戲中死亡就代表輸了。
特別規則
1) 抽牌時抽兩張留一張，另外一張直接進棄牌堆
2) 遇到需要其他玩家作決定時，損失一點story或destiny值，由自己做決定
3) 需要抽其他玩家的牌時，直接由牌堆中抽牌；若需要特別種類的牌，則抽到指定種類為止，其他都進棄牌堆
4) 要特別注意計算狀態的影響。若發生計算錯誤時，自行選擇story或destiny值作懲罰
5) 若你有skill到達master等級，當閱讀three award章節時可忽略mandatory段落。

RATING: 5
Fun game but with six players it took five hours, and we didn't even play to a full 20 points (15 instead). It was honestly like 30 minutes in between turns. It dragged on painfully long. I'd give it another go with two players, or three max. Another gripe is that story points and destiny points feel completely arbitrary, and also there is no advantage to weighing one more heavily over the other, but as a newbie there is no way to know this. Your goal in the game is to get a combination of 20 story and destiny points, and before the game you decide what that combination looks like (e.g. 10 of each, or 15 and 5, or 18 and 2) but it would never make sense to overly weight it to one side or the other. I'd say 12/8 max but really I can't envision why you wouldn't just always choose 10 and 10. I guess there is some mystery in that other players don't know which combination you've chosen, but other players also don't have much control over if and when you'll receive points, so it seems pointless. The best part about this game is the Choose Your Own Adventure aspect. That's the only thing that would bring me back to play it a second time.

RATING: 1
Looks really good, but after reading rules, I'll definitely need to try this one before buying it.

UPDATE- 

I am so glad I instituted a "try before I buy" policy on this "game". 

THIS IS A LONG FUCKING "GAME"

My first, and ONLY game- over 3.5 hours. I am so close to winning, and more importantly- ending it, at about 2 hours. I'm a turn away from returning to Baghdad and winning when- WHOOPS! I get an involuntary sex change as a result from an encounter. 

I'm thinking...so what, right? 

Except you can't win until YOUR SEX IS CHANGED BACK!  

So...I go back to a city where I have a card, that... IF I'M LUCKY and roll a Friggin' 3... I can then "Enter the Sex-Change Pool". 

I roll.

I ROLLED A 3! 

YES!!!

I choose to respond to the encounter by "Enter". Yes!...I want to ENTER the "Sex-Change Pool". Yes, please. 

At this point, the game has way way way overstayed its welcome for me and I really want it to end.

Anyway, something DIDN'T work out. I wasn't ABLE to enter the "Sex-Change Pool". Although I had ALL the right traits. 

But instead, and get this....I get to fill a flask with the water FROM the "Sex-Change Pool".

OK. Maybe this isn't so terrible...So then please, may I drink this water, so that it will change my sex, so I can win this damn game?

"No."

So...I can't win because I need a friggin' sex change. And...I'm now carrying around a flask with "Sex-Change" potion in it, but I can't friggin' drink it?

Fuck You, Arabian Nights. 

Every logical decision made in this game bestowed a completely illogical response from the system.

A shame, because I very much like story telling. I fully expected to really like this game.

This is not a game. 

And I want my four hours back.

RATING: 8
..Not much of a 'game', in the rawest sense.  More of an experience or RPG.  Quite literally, this is a choose-your-own-adventure board game.

On the positive side, this game is truly a wonderful experience.  All of the components are of the highest quality, the art is beautiful, and writing in the adventure book is fantastic - and clearly all of these things were laboriously considered and tweaked for the re-release.

On the negative side (and these are minor for me, but will be major to others) there are A LOT of cards, especially for statuses, that take up a large footprint on the boardgaming table.  The action choices don't really seem to line up with your skills (ie, if you come across an Angry Bandit and you were a swordsman with excellent skill in fighting, don't be surprised if you have to make a piety check in order to succeed an "attack" choice for the encounter).. this disconnect does get frustrating from time to time as players can easily come to learn that a) the skills that they want and want to improve will have little to do with the outcome of their action choices and b) the over arching and over powering randomness to the whole game.

Having said the above, I still enjoy this game.  I love the adventures that unfold and I love that despite playing the game 6 times there are still hundreds and hundreds (and hundreds?) of paragraphs, outcomes and locations that can still be explored.  Wonderful.
---

RATING: 8
Let's get this out of the way first: if you're looking for a game of applied strategy, go elsewhere. However, if you're looking for a storytelling experience that envelopes you and allows your imagination to run wild, you've come to the right place. 

Absolutely beautiful board and presentation in general. I can't say enough about this: I love setting this one up and diving into the world laying on the table in front of me. Gorgeous. The writing is high quality and done in a style that draws you into the exoticism of the lands and creatures you encounter.

It would be nigh impossible for every single choice and subsequent paragraph to be perfectly meshed, and there are times when the effect is disconnected to your choices. But even these can be fun if you approach the game knowing this in advance. Any choice can be appropriate and you always have an opportunity to be surprised and delighted (or frustrated) by the outcome.

If I could change something at all in the game, it would be this: I would have liked to see a few given attributes for the individual characters you play. Scheherazade, for instance, should automatically have a Storytelling skill, while Sinbad should be gifted with Seamanship from the outset. A minor quibble to be sure, but it makes sense to me. The long playing time can wear a bit as well, but I haven't really minded too much as I was enjoying the stories so much it didn't really matter. 

I love this game. Great fun with the right mindset and the right people. Another one that plays well solo as well.

RATING: 7
I've played a handful of what I call experience games - long duration, strong narrative, chaotic subsystems (e.g Lord of the Rings, Fury of Dracula, Marvel Superheroes) and this is the best so far for me, mainly because mechanically it is easy to get into: move (according to wealth level), encounter, choose response and award.

The other reason I like TotAN is the narrative is done the best way possible: verbally. The other experience games rely on unwieldy mechanics to stimulate the imagination, but I find it doesn't work as well as the spoken word.

Rating based on 1 play shortened to 12 victory points, which clocked in at 1/2 hour per player. Next time will implement the other short play rule of only 1 status in play as more is heady and longwinded for a first time players.

Update: one thing about playing games is that although the conditions of winning may be easy to comprehend, sometimes (or in my case often) the focus on how to reach that victory is difficult to achieve, especially if new mechanisms have to be mastered first. From my first play of TotAN I couldn't see how movement could contribute to victory, it appeared to be a random exercise, though the quest cards provided a distracting, short term goal. Then I examined the encounter deck and realised there is purpose to visit certain terrains.

So I've upped my rating because part of the game is about timing your movement to end on a terrain which will hopefully result in encountering the desired terrain encounter e.g lion in the desert. Obviously it helps in the decision making process if you know what the destiny/story awards will be but the refreshing joy about this design is the genuine feeling of exploring and discovery behind the real mystery of the Palace of a 1000 closets etc. Players can increase their chances of a desirable encounter by either rolling a 6 on the city cards or holding on to city cards to reduce the encounter deck, to recycle the desired terrain or character.

RATING: 7
I'm quite a fan of games that tell a story (although definitely not a role-player) and I like the book-driven mechanic here; the hundreds of paragraphs, made even more uncertain with the addition of the dice variable, ensures that no game is ever the same. RATING: 7
This was a really enjoyable game where you build a rudimentary character and then move around the board having adventures. The "goal" of the game is to get 20 points in some combination of points and then make it back to the city at the center of the board. The real goal of the game is to just have a wonderful time seeing where everyone's stories take them. The game is sort of a giant multiplayer choose your own adventure book, where each encounter gives you 5 or 10 different ways you can react to the situation. Sometimes, it can be a little ambiguous as to what you chose to do at any point, for example, I had an encounter with "Dark Creatures" and I looked at my options and saw I could pick "Aid". I was thinking this would be 'aid the dark creatures' but as it happens when the book was read I had actually chosen to aid the previously unmentioned victims of said dark creatures for which I was rewarded handsomely. Still fun, but maybe a little confusing. Another example was we had a player who encountered a small treasure. He was like, sure I'll take it, and chose "take". This lead to the book describing a scene of thievery that the player had not really intended. Despite this, everyone had a marvelous time getting into the stories that unfolded. Player's only real complaint was that with 6, the time between turns was a little too long. 

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:nostar: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::star::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 7
Tales of the Arabian Nights is a pure adventure game.

When a player takes a turn they move based on their wealth, and then have an encounter in the space they've landed by drawing a card.  These encounters can be everything from a disguised princess to a dark whirlpool (take my advice and DO NOT DRINK from the dark whirlpool).  There's some dice rolling and the player chooses how they want to respond to the encounter.  Finally another player reads the results of the encounter to them out of a huge book which literally contains thousands of possible outcomes.  Skills and possessions can affect the encounter's outcome.

Technically players are trying to acquire "story" and "destiny" points in order to win the game, and encounters almost always, no matter how bad, reward you with them.  You can also acquire wealth and new skills.  Or you may end up wounded, accursed, scorned, on a pilgramage, married, a vizier, the recipient of an unwanted sex-change and so on.  All these statuses affect how you can act on your turn.

Players start out with quests.  This gives the game a little bit of structure, but players are under no requirement to fulfill quests, though the quests often provide great rewards.  Winning the game is not the point.  The point of this game is to have grand and entertaining adventures.  The main source of player interaction is when one player reads aloud another's story.  It's a game version of telling stories around a campfire.  It's "Choose Your Own Adventure" the board game.  It's amazingly well crafted and brilliant.  

When an experienced palyer is teaching new players I would recommend having the neophytes just jump right in.  Most of the rules can be explained as they pop up.  I wouldn't recommend playing with more than four people.  The book of adventures is huge and it can take some time to find and read the various stories.  Players have to interrrupt their story for the other players to take turns.  It can be kind of annoying if you have to postpone your story long enough for five other people to experience the next chapter in their own.

Odd Note:  The insert is actually glued into the bottom of the box.

RATING: 4
[b]RO:[/b] [i]03/07/2016[/i]

This game is so cool. What? No, that isn't sarcasm. Yes, I know I rated the game a four. But it's true, this game is really cool. It just didn't pan out for my group... like, at all. You see, I'm really great at reading out loud (and no, that is not an "I am very smart" statement), [i]and[/i] more importantly, I think it's fun. Unfortunately, nobody else in my group thinks that (my wife outright refuses to do it, and one of my group is just really, really bad at it so we just end up having to ask him to let us read cards and such ourselves when he does). Even [i]more unfortunately[/i] that is basically this entire game. It has a board and tokens and "skills" but this is, at it's heart, a storytelling game. You move, you read a specific entry from a huge tome of possible scenarios and outcomes, you make your best guess at what you should do, and something good or bad happens. That's about it. But there is a lot of reading out loud, and I think this game lives or dies on a group's willingness to get into the story and [i]read aloud[/i]. My group doesn't much like either of those things, so Tales is pretty much going to sit on my shelf gathering dust, forever.

RATING: 9
I have never felt such pure delight as I did as I sat down to play my first game of Tales of the Arabian Nights.  I love the role-playing and the storytelling.  This game is so different from any other game I've played because there is very little to do in terms of strategy; it's only about laughing and enjoying while the game plays you.  Before I played Tales I would have thought that there isn't much to a game where it doesn't matter who wins.  Perhaps I still think that is true.  But that doesn't mean that this game isn't one of the best experiences I've ever had with a game.  It's pure fun.  I love the theme, the interactive game play and I love that there seems to be no downtime.  There is also no reason for AP.  In fact, I enjoy everyone else's turns as much as my own, which is a rare quality in a game.  It doesn't hurt that the game itself is beautiful--the board, the cards and all of the art is superb.

RATING: 7.5
Maybe it's just because I come from a role-playing background, but I found Arabian Nights to be highly enjoyable. There really isn't much game here. It's mostly a choose-your-own-adventure book with A LOT of random consequences. I think this game would be a little better if if the skills you earn were more closely aligned with the choices you make throughout the game. For example, you would think having the Piety skill would result in the Pray action succeeding more often, but I haven't found that to be the case.

The natural comparison here is Agents of SMERSH, which perfects the skill/choice system, in my opinion. That being said, I think Arabian Nights is still better at the story-telling aspect. Best for a lazy afternoon with a few friends. Overall: lots of fun and a ton of laughs as long as you're willing to let the game lead you by the hand.

RATING: 8
Rated 8 after first play.  Lots of fun, but think that there would be too much downtime in a game with 4 or more players.

Update : after playing some more I really like this game.  I will say it is very "mood/group dependent".  If you have 3 people that enjoy a casual/noncompetative game and have a little bit of a RPG'er in them, they should have a great time.  It can be a bit long if you play to the recommended 20 point total/combo.  If you think the patience of the players might wear thin after 2 hours, you may want to knock the victory condition down to 16 or less.

I also think it is important to let new players know that they need to relax and let the story flow.  If they are too concerned with winning or losing, or if they are prone to frustration if "bad" things happen to their character (and they will) they will likely not enjoy the game.  Player selection is key for an enjoyable experience.

EDIT: I have tried it with a number of casual gamers and it was not very popular.  Weird - it seems a natural match.  But looking up paragraphs is fairly slow.  I would suggest that you play with a maximum of 3 players.  I am dropping my ratings by a point to 5.

RATING: 8
A game that plays very differently from any other game that I played before. Much reading of text results in many distinct brief adventure episodes. It is a great formula that appeals to everyone I have played it with so far. Lots of luck and, especially at game start, not much chance of influencing outcomes, but this improves in your favour as the game progresses and your abilities increase.

When this game was published I was not sure if it would be my cup of tea, and the price tag prevented me from trying it out. When it was later offered for a reasonable price (by Dutch standards) I jumped on it, and I am glad that I did. Also because I apparently got the second print run with the minor errors already corrected. Good production quality too. This was a very good buy that is likely to hit the table often.

Initial rating after 3 plays: 8 out of 10, with the potential to become a 9.

RATING: 4

Well, the stories are genuinely intriguing but it takes so much busywork to actually get there, even when using an app to assist in page-selection.
Meanwhile the roleplaying is handled in a classic choose your own adventure-style, which i usually love, but so often your choices will lead to unexpected outcomes, leaving you feeling like you have almost no agency in the game. 
In my opinion, play dnd or any other rpg instead, you'll have a much better time.

RATING: 6.03
This reminded me a lot of the Steve Jackson books I use to read many moons ago.  Not so much a game, as an adventure.  Each turn you move your character to new destinations within Europe, Africa & the Middle East.  You then draw an event card and a story unravels before you.  From a pre-scripted selection you can then make a choice on how your character deals with the event.
It is certainly different from any game previously played.  Highly luck driven, its a game not for everyone or indeed every occasion.  
Its more like an expensive Christmas whisky, something to be savoured and enjoyed on those rare occasions when friends get together and want to just enjoy each others company.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I like that it is a unique adventure game. While it does not reward creativity, it provides a narrative which resembles the original stories and language style. I like that if you use your skills to back up your choices, you tend to fair better. The destiny die throws curve balls at your plans every so often.

This is not a strategy game and winning seems to come from a combination of factors and good choices is only one of them. Victory feels a little more than a timer to let everyone know that the story has come to a conclusion. 

I like TotAN for what it is. A light adventure game in a unique setting.

I still think your best bet is to roleplay your character. Choose skills that make sense together. If you have weapon use, attack people. If you prefer to talk, choose seduction, beguiling, Courtly Graces/Storytelling. If you want to explore, Enduring Hardship, Wilderness Lore and Wisdom or Magic are good bets. 

Grouping the skills is important because sometimes it is ambiguous which one will be used for a specific choice and the Destiny Die may also shift what skill will be used.

This is not a strategic game but it is not mindlessly random either.

RATING: 8
This would be a perfect ten if they would revise the encounter book to make sense.  No more being in a desert and having an "ocean" encounter!  Also, they desperately need to fix such problems as:
- players should NOT be able to spend three hours in jail in one game.
- players should be more able to get rid of curses
- there should be a GRAND END ENCOUNTER to wrap up the "story" instead of an abrupt, random "oh, I've got points, the end, I win."  This is too abrupt and jarring and is just a let down in such a thematic game.  

Agents of Smersh puts this game to shame, and yet...I love Tales of the Arabian Nights.  I hope they make it right someday.

RATING: 4
This game needs an app. Looking up the encounters is just too fiddly, not to mention passing that book *and* lookup chart back and forth. The encounters slowly become more exotic as the the players gain skills. But the usually negative Status conditions result in players not moving around the board and otherwise losing "half a turn". Like other adventure boardgames (eg. Talisman), players almost never interact, so this is best a two-player game.

*****

CYOA. Riiight.

(After spending way too long looking up card icons and making dice modifiers)

Me: The sultan seeks a bride! Do you compete for his affections?
Ian: I have the Seduction skill. That should work. Yes.
Me: You have wed the sultan! After years of happiness, his mother arrives and challenges you to a duel. Do you have the Bulgarian Basket Weaving skill?
Ian: Um... no.
Me: She turns you into a newt.

Well, that's what it felt like. And Ian was buried twice because he got married. :laugh:

RATING: 7
This game is not for everyone, as it centers in the story that is told and not in the competition between players.  So, hardcore gamers may not like this game, as well as other people that simple don't like stories or that aren't very imaginative.  Besides, for non English speakers playing the game is nearly impossible.  Having said that, I think it is a very good game which departs from a very interesting concept, centered around the amazing way that they've tied all the paragraphs together to form coherent stories. The productions values are one of the highest in the market.  You can see all the care and heart behind the game, specially the Book of Tales.  It is my first Z-Man Game, but I can tell you it won't be the last, if they put this kind of effort in all the games that they make.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
"Tales of the Arabian Nights" might tell a good story, but that doesn't mean it's a good game. I found it to be overly random to the point of being chaotic. So much of the game is out of your control as a player due the cards, statuses, and story elements. It's also possible to have lost (or at least significantly put yourself behind) before the game even starts when you need to decide your own win condition by splitting 20 points between the two types of points in the game (story and destiny).  The problem with this is that you have absolutely no idea how to specifically earn each type of point in the game since both are more or less randomly awarded throughout the game game.  So, if you are in the position that you need more story points than destiny points to win, what choice do you make on the next encounter to get more store points?  The reality is that your choice of action doesn't matter because there is no way to know which type of points you'll randomly get during any particular encounter.  Arbitrarily picking a win condition and earning those points in a completely random, unpredictable way does not make for a very fun game experience.  Choosing your own adventure sounds like you have some control in the game, but you really don't.  It gets even worse since there are so many things that put your actions in the hands of other players and there is nothing that you can do about that at all.  On top of that, the game run 30-45 minutes per player and although parts of the story can be funny, the overall story is really quite disjointed.  In the end, the game is too long, with too much randomness and very little control afforded to the player.  This is more of a frustrating activity with moments of laughter than it is an actual game.

RATING: 8
Streamlined version of the original.  Quality is improved in most every way, but there are some minor issues that I have with it.  

First, As much as I like the map (and I do like the map for the most part), it just doesn't compare with the original.  This is an aesthetical choice of course, but hey, this is an experience game so it is important.  

Second, in the original, statuses don't stack, so if you get a new status the old one goes away.  This means that you don't automatically lose when you get sex change or stay insane forever never getting control, etc.  This can always be ruled in.

I'm disappointed that they didn't update Blessing and Respected, these have always slowed the original game down and felt anti-thematic to me.  They are really dated mechanics and even in their day made for too much downtime.  That said, we still are at an age where Arkham Horror thinks it is fine to dig through the deck to find a card you want...sigh.

I really like the quest cards in this version.  They are a great refinement.  I think they are imbalanced and should be able to be traded in for new ones in Bagdad, but I like them.  

All in all this is a great edition. I'd pick it up if I didn't already have the original.

RATING: 9
What more needs to be said about TotAN? Too many of the "modern" games that I see hitting the tables these days are little more than heartless mechanical exercises that ultimately have little function other than to provide a reason for 2-6 people to sit around a table to see who can follow the rules most efficiently. TotAN is the exact opposite of that style of gaming - it's passionate, colorful, enchanting, and one of a kind, full of surprises and outrageous fortune. A multiplayer choose-your-own-adventure game rooted in the mythos of the 1001 Arabian Nights and an Ameritrash classic to boot? Yes please.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played with the right group, this can be a gaming experience you will never forget - mind you though - with the right group. There is a great deal of reading passages in a hefty book, so, not for folks who don't read english well, or those who do not enjoy reading. The stories should also be read with flair, so monotone readers may put a damper on things. The other aspect that may kill this game for some is that it is largely a random romp through Luckland. Truly, the choices you make, no matter how logical they seem, will certainly influence the outcome of the situation, but for good or ill is anyone's guess. Your character may have all the necessary skills that fit a situation perfectly, but there are times when not having the skill would leave you better off. So why strive for developing a character with more skills and more experience when those attributes may not actually help, and may certainly hinder your character? Well, because when playing this game one must simply let go. Let go of all the notions of traditional strategy games and tactical euros. Let go of competition and logic - and instead ready yourself for a fun, chaotic, funny ride where if your character becomes lost, or assumes beast form, who cares? If it's good for the story, then it's good for the game. Just let go and enjoy the ride - it may not make sense, but it certainly makes for a joyful time.

RATING: 8.2
BestWith:3>4_Not5,6.
Variant Day 1: Roll the +/0/- die for Morning Noon Night.
Variant Day 2: Or use a 10 sided die: 1-4 Morning, 5-7 Noon, 8-9 Night, 10 Roll Again.
Variant Day 3: Or base Morning/Noon/Night on someone getting DP+SP 7 for Noon, 14 for Night?
Variant 4: For faster start each player starts with 4 skills. Perhaps award another skill at SP=7?
Variant 5: You can only be in jail for a maximum of 3 turns, then you can pay story or destiny to get out.
Variant 6: Don't select specific goals for Story and Destiny points. Just figure a pre-determined amount (20 in a 3-4 player game) and allow someone to trigger the end if they get to that many total.
Variant 7: When you have an encounter with artwork based on a land type, you may keep that card (up to 3) and draw another encounter. Thereafter once in that land type you can encounter that card.
Variant 8: Limit status to 3. Over that and you may discard one.

RATING: 6
To me, this game is unnecessarily cumbersome. To provide a vaguely linear "story," it depends on players having to consult and cross-reference and adjust various matrices; this gives you some choices to make but really a simple instantaneous draw of some kind of Event card would have sufficed and been much less fiddly, more than making up for whatever minor loss of continuity you might suffer. Then there are the many status modifiers you must keep track of (a personal pet peeve of mine)...it's not a bad game, just a little too clunky to work as a free-wheeling adventure. When you have so little control of what becomes of you, maybe it's best to make things easier and quicker.

RATING: 2
While the story that unfolds before you can be very entertaining, this game has many drawbacks. The effects that are often applied to your character can be debilitating and very hard to overcome.  Game time is way too long as well.  There could be so much to improve in this game and make it an enjoyable experience.
