RATING: 5.5
The thing that strikes me first is that I cannot even begin to imagine how much work went into creating this game!  It is fairly epic!  My rating is based on a single 3-player game that I played in order to get the rules down, which was easy enough to do since it just takes a turn or 2 to get into the flow of the game.  I had read about how random this game is and how it is simply a story-telling adventure, but that was okay with me since my wife and I love stories and adventures!  However, even with fairly low expectations I was still disappointed.  It really is INCREDIBLY random, to the point where you feel like there are almost no meaningful decisions to make since every reaction to any situation could end up being good or bad, or both.  It's like being in an out-of-control car, but without the excitement.  And even though I approached it as being similar to those "choose your own adventure" books I loved as a kid, I still felt let down because there is nothing linear or story-like about the adventure - it's just one random event after the next.  I can see why 3 players is the ideal number (and I wouldn't want to play with more than 4) since the game would really start to drag otherwise.  But having said all that, the production value is excellent and the components look very nice (yay - a blue sea on the map!).  My wife seemed to enjoy the game more than myself, and I would definitely be willing to give it another try now that I know what to expect.  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 1.5/5  Total: 5.5/10

RATING: 6
Highly thematic and highly random storytelling game. Players take the role of one of the characters from 'The Arabian Nights' and manoeuvre them around a point to point map of Asia, North Africa and southern Europe. When landing on spaces a card is drawn revealing a particular type of encounter specific to that type of space. Players then decide on how to react to that encounter based on a number of options. The result of the encounter is then obtained from an impressively comprehensive list in the game's 'storybook'. Points are awarded depending on the outcome of the encounter and the winner is determined by reaching a certain number of points on two tracks and a player being able to return their character to Baghdad. 'TotAN' is an incredibly random game, in fact it isn't really a game in the traditional sense, whereby you have some control over what happens. As the player you just have to make a guess as to which action will lead to the most favourable outcome for you. This is therefore a game that should never be played with anyone who insists on there being a strong competitive element to proceedings. With a group who understand what the game is designed to do, namely tell a story, and who aren't troubled by the notion of winning and losing, this can be a highly enjoyable experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.2
I don't really know how to rate this game as it almost isn't a game. People had explained to me before that it is essentially a 'choose your own experience' book with a board attached. You make some arbitrary decisions and 'stuff' happens.

Having now played it, the choices aren't as abstract as they were sold to me; if you have a few skills (you start with 3) the options available to you in each encounter can be tailored towards the skills you have which will tend you give you more positive results than randomly picking an action. However, the pull to choose the silly or funny option is often overwhelming and the game might punish you a little for doing so, but it probably won't guarantee you lose the game.

It's actually one you can play to win, but it almost (almost!) doesn't matter. It really is an experience - and that's from a Eurogamer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
"Not the destination, but the journey" game. If you want to have a relaxed, fun time then this game provides it. Especially recommended for couples.

This is neither a cooperative  nor a competitive game. There are winning conditions, but I believe they are just there for the purpose of ending the game. You can set a time limit instead, and it creates the same experience. 

==Not for you if==

If you can't get out of a competitive mindset while playing board games, or if you only enjoy competitive games then this game is probably not for you.

I believe people who criticize the game with the argument "your choices don't matter" are the ones who are playing it to win. Your choices do matter, different things happen to you based on your choices. It's just that they might not work the way you expect them to work (it's a fairy tale land after all). This probably frustrates the people who are playing it to win. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 5
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 1

RATING: 7
Unique and gorgeous component wise, the stories are coherent and if you pay attention and choose actions that are related to your abilities you will definitely have much more success, which proves this wasn't just designed willy-nilly.  It's fun and unique and even in 2017 I don't think another game has really done the story based game better.

That said the rules seem half baked on several points, I don't mean that there is ambiguity just that they seem...stupid?  For instance only cycling the morning/day/night counter when you finish the encounter deck?  What?  You'd have to play absolutely horribly and for over 5 hours to get to night time...it will legitimately never happen.  The win condition rules seem arbitrary and at the same time too easy to obtain as well.

In essence the game lends itself to several house rules to improve the experience, which I have to hold against the game a little bit.

RATING: 5
Not exactly what I was expecting. More Bethesda's Elder Scrolls than Bioware's Baldur's Gate...meaning you make your own story from a series of disjointed mini adventures rather than have a linear cohesive story told to you. Often short paragraphs that aren't engaging or meaningful to your skillset or reactions and many rules issues, which can be easily house-ruled or ignored. Played very similar to World of Warcraft Adventure Game to me. This is NOT "Choose your own adventure" in the sense of those books because it's not telling one story. Your choices are generally only to choose a random location to stop in (often with no rhyme or reason to go there) and then to choose a reaction, such as pray, rob, honor, examine, hire, etc. One paragraph or mini adventure does not lead you to the next or continue your story or experience. Generally the paragraph will result in you gaining a point or two and possibly taking on a "status," which can be good or bad. Travel to another random blip and repeat. This is OK for what it is. If you want more of a single story that has all the "Choose your own adventure" elements as in those books or the Microquest Death Tests, try Cutthroat Caverns: Tombs and Tomes.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[thing=34119][/thing] is a pretty simple game: the characters travel in a map, having encounters in its several locations, and in each of them the player chooses what the character will do from a set of options: for instance, Aladdin encounters a Wicked Enchantress, and the game informs that to deal with her Aladdin must use one option from the table B, which has: Grovel, Aid, Rob, Avoid, Bargain, Attack, Court, Trick, Hire. Then, lets say I choose "Court", since my Aladdin has, as skills, Seduction, Storytelling, Appearence and other that might be useful (in the beginning of the game, every character starts with 3 skills from 18 possible).

After this, is simply a matter to cross the choice made (Court) with the "Wicked" line in a table of the letter (B) given in the encounter. This will give a number to go in the big book of encounters - for example, say it is 1609. Then, the player rolls the Destiny die, which can make the encounter happens one number bellow (1608), one number up (1610) or in the actual number given (1609) - if the player has a "golden" skill (meaning that the character is a master in that skill) and this skill appears in one of the three possible encounters (bellow, the same or above), the encounter that will happen will be the one that uses this master skill. In the reference of the encounter is written what happened and there are, usually, two possibilities: in the case the character didn't have a required skill or item that the encounter requires, the player reads the "No skill" part; if the character do have the skill or item required in the encounter, it is read the part that takes this into consideration - and, of course, in this way the character gets the best results. Once the encounter is over, normally the turn passes to the next player. And the game goes like this until it ends.

The encounters give, usually, one or more of four things: Story points, Destiny points, Treasures and/or Conditions. The Story and Destiny points are what makes the game end and a winner appears (at the start of the game, every player must divide 20 points between Story and Destiny anyway he wants, like 10 / 10, or 13 / 7) - when the character reaches the amount of points chosen in Story and Destiny, the characters must reach Bagdah in order to win. Treasures are pretty useful items. Conditions can be good or bad, like Married, Chased, Cursed, Transformed, Fated, Scorned, Respected, and several others, and each has their own special rules.

Besides walk around the world and get stuff, the characters can also travel in order to fulfill Quests, and each has their own set of rules and rewards when completed. After a Quest is fulfilled the player receives another, and is in the best interest of the player to try to complete these Quests, since they give a lot of good things, and also provide a sense of direction to go with the character instead of just wandering around the map, whithout a bigger purpose. Besides this, some encounters give the player city cards, and aftwerwards, when the character reaches the said city, after the encounter there, you can discard the city card and roll a d6 to see what will happen with the character there (usually you can gain or lose items, or have another an encounter there).

[thing=34119][/thing] showed itself as a fun  game, providing many memorable situations throughout the whole play. But a few warnings are necessary: first, the game comes with a [u]huge[/u] downtime - between turns, the other players usually have nothing do to besides listen to the encounters of others (eventually one are required to make some choices for the other player). This truly makes BGG correct in the "best player count", since three seems to me to be spot on to reduce the downtime to a more manageable amount. [thing=34119][/thing] with 5 or 6 persons will probably a nightmare in the downtime front.

Second, is important that everyone knows that the game isn't really a competition. If you want to strategize and likes control, maybe [thing=34119][/thing] isn't a game for you. The results of the encounters are unpredictable, even if you try to make the best use of the skill of your character - for example, in one situation, Ali Baba's player choose to Attack some evil creatures, since Ali Baba had Weapon Use and Magic, so he thought he had some bases covered, but, once the encounter happened, he needed to do a trap, using Wilderness Lore, which he didn't had, so Ali Baba was beaten and scorned. Not even making conservative choices (accordingly to the situation or the skills your character has) guarantee any sort of better success rating than just playing almost at random. Also, in some situations, it is the other players that decide things for you (such as which places is necessary to visit in order to fulfill a Quest), and, if the game is seen as a competition, it would be in your best interest to put the places as far and wide as possible, to make the completion of it pretty hard. But the game loses with this, since it is way more fun to actually see the end of the Quest, to see the effects of it, specially because these tend to be the most thought out and fun encounters to have.

[thing=34119][/thing] is a game that has a place mainly of its own: its is not a game of competition, but is also not a cooperative; it is a game in which the main thing are the stories told, but it isn't a storytelling game (you don't have to have savvy in telling stories on the spot, like in [thing=18723][/thing] or [thing=1234][/thing], nor do you have much control on the story as you have in [thing=12692][/thing]); it is a game without great demands in rules, with low complexity, though it isn't as simple or direct as, say, [thing=39856][/thing]. So it is possible to trace several parallels with other games, but none that can embrace the whole extension of [thing=34119][/thing].

Therefore, [thing=34119][/thing] is certainly not a game for everyone (it is long, with severe downtime, and, in the quibbles department, the box is huge and weights like if it is full of rocks, and this is mainly due to the big book of encounters, lots of cards and the thick board), but is indeed, certainly, an unique game, capable of creating stories that will be remembered for a long time.

RATING: 5
In Tales of the Arabian Nights, you are the hero or heroine in a story of adventure and wonder just like those told by Scheherazade to her spellbound sultan! You will travel the land seeking your own destiny and fortune. You will learn stories and gain wisdom to share with others. Will you be the first to fulfill your destiny? The next Tale is yours to tell! There is, of course, a winner in Tales of the Arabian Nights, but the point of the game is less to see who wins and more to enjoy the unfolding and telling of a great story!
In this new edition of the groundbreaking storytelling game, you enter the lands of the Arabian Nights alongside Sindbad, Ali Baba, and the other legendary heroes of the tales. Travel the world encountering imprisoned princesses, powerful 'efreets, evil viziers, and such marvels as the Magnetic Mountain and the fabled Elephant's Graveyard.
Choose your actions carefully and the skills you possess will reward you: become beloved, wealthy, mighty - even become sultan of a great land. Choose foolishly, however, and become a beggar, or be cursed with a beast's form or become insane from terror! YOU will bring to life the stories of the inestimable Book of Tales in this vastly replayable board game with over 2002 tales that will challenge, amuse, astound and spellbind you for years to come.

RATING: 8
The pinnacle of narrative-driven boardgaming.  This is not so much a game as an experience, a story that is told using the medium of board game.  The vagaries of fate are everpresent, and the amount of strategy is effectively nil.  There is no such right choice to make at any point in the game, because they are all just as likely to screw you as to help you.  Nevertheless, this is a rollicking ride, true to its source material, and you have a great story when its over.

With too many players, the game is nearly unplayable.  The freshness of the game lags after a couple hours, and the game becomes very dull.  I cap it at five players, and suggest four.  The nature of the game means that I would love to play with a larger number of people, preferably with liberal application of alcohol, but it just gets too bogged down.  Swashbuckling, story-driven games should be able to handle large groups, dammit!

RATING: N/A
In Tales of the Arabian Nights, you are the hero or heroine in a story of adventure and wonder just like those told by Scheherazade to her spellbound sultan! You will travel the land seeking your own destiny and fortune. You will learn stories and gain wisdom to share with others. Will you be the first to fulfill your destiny? The next Tale is yours to tell! There is, of course, a winner in Tales of the Arabian Nights, but the point of the game is less to see who wins and more to enjoy the unfolding and telling of a great story!
\13\\13\
In this new edition of the groundbreaking storytelling game, you enter the lands of the Arabian Nights alongside Sindbad, Ali Baba, and the other legendary heroes of the tales. Travel the world encountering imprisoned princesses, powerful 'efreets, evil viziers, and such marvels as the Magnetic Mountain and the fabled Elephant's Graveyard.
\13\\13\
Choose your actions carefully and the skills you possess will reward you: become beloved, wealthy, mighty - even become sultan of a great land. Choose foolishly, however, and become a beggar, or be cursed with a beast's form or become insane from terror! YOU will bring to life the stories of the inestimable Book of Tales in this vastly replayable board game with over 2002 tales that will challenge, amuse, astound and spellbind you for years to come.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a giant multi-player choose-your-own adventure story. Despite the fact that you sit down for three hours, draw some cards and move things around on a giant board, you are not actually playing a game. There's no way to control what is happening to you or to position yourself for a win. Now that I think about it, even the victory conditions themselves are fairly random. Yet it's still a lot of fun.

The random things that happen to you are often quite funny. Especially once you are packing around some status and quest cards. I mean, what's funnier than the Prince of Thieves being transformed into a sex-changed donkey looking for love in the slave market? It's also hard not to be impressed with the sheer effort that went into writing the game's enormous adventure book. It's a pretty incredible piece of game design.

I don't always know what to do with Tales of the Arabian Nights. I never feel like I've gotten a satisfying "game" out of it, but I've never had a bad time playing either. I'll probably never get off the fence on it...so I guess it'll just have to hang around. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An enjoyable experience. This isn't really much of a "game" in the sense of most of the other highly ranked games here; there are more interesting decisions than I thought there would be, but overall, it seems to be the sort of thing where you sit back and enjoy the story. Choose your own adventure with a board seems like an apt description. But, it is a good time, and plays very quickly (my first 2 player game took an hour and a half including rules explanation), which means it doesn't wear out its welcome. My biggest concern going into this was that it would be like an inferior version of Once Upon A Time where the players have no freedom, but I was pleased to discover a totally different type of game. My initial impression is that it rewards sticking to your strengths (we'll see if that holds up over repeated plays, or if I was just lucky). I don't think it's something I'd want to play all the time, but this definitely has a place in large game collections.

RATING: 7
Fun idea, nice components overall, and nice as a non-competitive activity. But way too much downtime for 5+, maybe 4+.

The initial paras. (1-150 or so) should have been consolidated with the matrix lookup guide instead of in the main story book.

More conditional paragraphs and "random result" paragraphs would have been better, esp. with the more likely earlier encounters. This is just so obvious it's painful.

Fewer paragraphs but more relating to skill usage (so the same amount overall, just cut up differently) would have been better.  As is, skills often feel irrelevant, when in fact they should differentiate your story from everyone else's. Which leads to....

Why does Ali Baba = Aladdin = Sindbad = Scheherezade = Zumurrud = Ma'Aruf?  Given that quests are integrated so tightly, why not give them each a starting quest (or three) that determines a couple of starting skills, some backstory, etc.?  Feels too generic as is.

RATING: 9
Most of what you need to know about this game is that it has a thin manual, and a 1" thick book of story scenarios that the players respond to.

The game is very easy to learn and interact with, but more than that, if you have the slightest roleplaying bone in your body, it allows you to insert yourself into a character in Arabia.  

Choices are rarely this-or-that.  You have TONS of options, and you can pretty much always find one that fits the character that you are building.  

I've gone into a game deciding to be a righteous and helpful man, but when the game stripped me of my piety skill, it was natural that I should also change the character to become jaded and angry with the world!

This is the kind of game that creates stories that you will tell to other people later.
And it does that because it gives you incredible agency, while not giving you 100% control over your life.  

How many games can you play for 90-120 min (with three players) and think:
"wow, i want to play again"

There are a few things with the game that are in no way deal breakers, but could be better:
* There isn't a ton of interaction between the players and characters
* The game DRAGS if there are 4-6 players. Play it with 2-3 if you can. 
* You need to find out if the players are going to be ok with the harsh nature of the early game, and maybe soften it if all the bad things happening to them at once are going to make them frustrated, rather than just laughing.

This game is magical.

RATING: 8
An experience more than a game, this is one of my very favorites.  I absolutely LOVE playing Tales of the Arabian Nights!  A  huge bonus is that my daughter does also.

Winning or losing has no bearing on gameplay.  No strategy will help you because you're at the mercy of kings, paupers, wizards, witches, dozens of creatures and rolls of the dice.

Gameplay goes like this:

Roll--Move to a new location--Have an encounter with a person or creature--Decide how you are going to interact and/or respond to them--Cross your fingers in hopes that you aren't crippled or cursed in some way.

It is a laugh-fest whenever I play it.  The situations are unique, light-hearted, and always seem to ground my game goals into sand.

It does take a bit of time to set up and can take awhile to play, especially if you are playing with four or more players, but it can be worth it.

A great family game.  Opens up the imagination and guaranteed to bring many laughs to gaming night.  Perfect for holiday get-togethers.  :)

RATING: 6
As long as you're happy laughing at the misfortunes of others (like me), this game/social event is entertaining for an hour or so.  After that, I just want it to end.

From my perspective, fantasy-minded families are really the best possible audience for this genre of game.  Unfortunately, in the case of Tales, some of the paragraph text and player actions - even if they're just silly fun for adults - are too dark for young kids.

RATING: 4
This is essential a competitive choose your own adventure game, but each player is working on their adventure separate from the other players, so there is very little interaction between the characters (although one character did pay to pass on her herpes and grief to another player).  The interaction lies in the heckling by the players.

I'd give it another go, but I was hoping for more of a story game, akin to 'Once upon a time.'  When the huge book came out, i knew I was wrong.  

This is certainly not a game to be taken very seriously, and is likely best played with plenty of beer, no, wine, on hand.  You can make what seem like great choices, but then the handsome barmaid mends you over a barrel and has her way with your backside, or you can try to what seems like suicide, but the merman respects your designs to push his people below the poverty line, and composes songs about you for the effort.

At first play, it was a crapshoot, but I wasn't bored for it.

RATING: 4
It's like if a choose-your-own-adventure had you turn to a random page every time, and occasionally would say "if you've been to page 33 already, read this instead."

I'm fine with the chaos of the gameplay itself, but the storytelling element is also chaotic, and getting an interesting story is just as likely as having nothing happen and ending up stuck in prison, getting the same story every time. It's propped up as a good example of emergent narrative in games but the lack of player agency means I don't care what is being read to me either.

I feel like there are things to be salvaged from this game that other games could borrow, but gosh I hate playing it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
How this gets the praise it does I'll never understand. An exercise in random, disjointed nothingness. I loved the idea, but the execution was far removed from what I imagined. None of your decisions made any difference, and story threads weren't linked at all. So glad I didn't shell out for it as I had planned to.
